China wants one of its biggest tech unicorns to go public at home.

Authorities have asked smartphone maker Xiaomi Corp. to list its shares on the mainland, according to people familiar with the situation, who say the company is considering doing so with a dual listing. It already plans a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/xiaomi-plots-a-path-out-of-the-wilderness-with-a-big-ipo-1513933772".

Beijing-based Xiaomi aims to raise at least $10 billion, according to people familiar with the matter, which would make the IPO one of the largest globally this year. The company is targeting a valuation of $80 billion to $100 billion, one of the people said.

Unicorns are private companies with valuations above $1 billion. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinas-xiaomi-raises-over-1-billion-in-investment-round-1419093589".

A Xiaomi spokeswoman declined to comment. Representatives of the China Securities Regulatory Commission didn't return requests for comment.

A dual listing would be a win for Chinese regulators, which have been trying to get large homegrown companies to participate in the country's capital markets. A string of tech giants have listed their shares elsewhere: internet giants Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and Baidu Inc. in New York and Tencent Holdings Ltd. in Hong Kong.

There have been few concurrent dual listings by Chinese companies that included their home market. The most recent was in 2010, when state-owned "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052748704535004575349210680027260", according to Dealogic data.

Some market participants and analysts say regulators could encourage more technology companies by loosening listing rules. Currently only companies that have been profitable for three consecutive years, or that obtain special approval from a state council, can apply to go public on the Shanghai or Shenzhen stock exchange.

Foxconn Industrial Internet Co., a subsidiary of Taiwan-based electronics contract manufacturer Foxconn Technology Group that was established less than three years ago, recently won approval from the state council to seek a Shanghai IPO.

The participants and analysts say securities regulators could also expedite their IPO review processhich currently takes as long as 18 monthsor companies that are industry leaders.

After a meeting in early January with internet, ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 and biotech companies, Zhang Shenfeng, assistant chairman of the China Securities Regulatory Commission, said the agency aims to "keep the good companies at home" and allow them to go public as soon as possible, according to a statement. Three weeks later, commission Chairman Liu Shiyu said at a conference that China's listing rules could be changed to help support more technology and new-economy companies.

A dual listing could benefit Xiaomi. The company, founded in 2010, sells its phones mostly in China, and could draw interest from the many domestic investors familiar with its brand.

Xiaomi has increased its global smartphone market share by expanding into new markets such as India and Spain. In fiercely competitive China, it "https://www.wsj.com/articles/xiaomi-plots-a-path-out-of-the-wilderness-with-a-big-ipo-1513933772", trailing Huawei Technologies Co., Oppo Electronics Corp. and Vivo Electronics Corp, according to Counterpoint Research.

But after declining in 2016, Xiaomi's China market share rose last year, and its overall smartphone shipments were up more than 50% to 96 million units, according to Counterpoint. And Xiaomi has been broadening its offerings beyond phones to include internet-connected devices from rice cookers to air purifiers.

Xiaomi doesn't publicly report financial results, but a spokeswoman previously said annual revenue exceeds 100 billion yuan ($15.2 billion). In the U.S., Xiaomi's product line is currently limited to connected devices like Bluetooth speakers and fitness trackers, but it has plans to sell smartphones eventually.

CMO Today: WPP's Weak Earnings Report; Spotify's IPO; NBCU Cuts Ad Time; Here's your morning roundup of the biggest marketing, advertising and media industry news and happenings.### invalid font number 1

Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Good morning. Marketing departments often conjure up new specialist roles so their companies can get their heads around the latest trends and technologies. Remember everyone rushing to appoint a "head of mobile" and "digital evangelists"? Here's a new one: "http://www.thedrum.com/news/2018/02/28/bank-america-creates-brand-safety-officer-role-help-it-clean-up-house". The new job was born out of a necessity to clean house. The bank's senior vice president and enterprise media executive Lou Paskalis said at Mobile World Congress earlier this week: "I get a text from my chief financial officer every time there is news about a brand safety issue. I know why he is sending them to me... at some point he is going to say 'gee, is marketing safe to invest in?' and we don't want that."

Oh You Pretty Things

WPP rounded off the agency holding company earnings season with its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wpp-shares-plunge-on-worst-results-in-years-1519894944", which summed up a fairly shoddy 12 months across the sector. In the earnings release, Mr. Sorrell said, "2017 for us was not a pretty year." He blamed cost-cutting by clients engaged in zero-based budgeting and technological changes as the main contributors to the company's decline in full-year sales. WPP also warned that 2018 would be a slog, too, and set budgets on the assumption that both revenue and net sales will be flat. The antidote? "Horizontality": WPP's effort to simplify its vast structure of specialist shops and get its agencies working better together on client projects. We've already seen the consolidation of agencies such as the formation of Wavemaker in its media division and yesterday's merger of Burson- Marsteller and Cohn & Wolfe in the PR vertical. The company is also "horizontalizing" (they said it, not me) its internal systems, announcing today, for example, its production management platform Hogarth will now be rolled out for the entire company to use.

ediapawhoza?

Rival agency groups have suggested 2018 may have a repeat of 2015's "Mediapalooza," when a large number of clients called media reviews. But Mr. Sorrell doesn't see a Mediapalooza 2 on the horizon, saying that while there has been increased new business activityajor reviews going on at the moment include Mars, Shell and HSBCt's not particularly busier than any other time. WPP Finance Chief Paul Richardson even said that the $21 billion of media billings on the table three years ago wasn't dramatically more than the $18 billion that usually changes hands in a normal year. What *is* significant, according to Mark Read, CEO of WPP's Wunderman, is that a tremendous amount of the activity this time is driven by companies looking at data and how they organize their tech stack, including how they work with companies like Adobe and Salesforce.

Raising the Roof

We finally have a look under the hood of Spotify's business after the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/spotify-kicks-off-its-unusual-ipo-1519847272", WSJ reports. Lots to absorb from an advertising perspective. Spotify had 92 million monthly users on its ad-supported service this year, up 19% on 2016. It had 159 million monthly users in total. (Interesting that a monthly active user is counted if they consume content for "greater than zero" millisecondshat's one very short song!) Ad-supported revenue grew 41% to ¬16 million ($507 million) in 2017, which the company said was due to an increase in the number of impressions sold, driven largely by the growth in its programmatic channel. Spotify also managed to boost its average rate per impression by 7%, buoyed by the growth of its premium video offering. Elsewhere in the filing, Spotify says 62% of its users are in the coveted 18- to 34-year-old demo, and that there's a big opportunity to grow its user base by gaining share from the $28 billion global radio market.

This Is the Next Century, Where NBCUniversal's Free (Of Ads?)

Typical! A week after I suggested that the smattering of news about TV companies experimenting with reducing their ad minutage doesn't look to be escalating into a widespread trend, another network group makes a big announcement about cutting down their commercials. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nbcu-cuts-ad-time-in-original-prime-time-shows-by-10-1519837249" across its networks, CMO Today reports. NBCU also wants to reduce the number of ads per break by 20%. The network hopes to offset the reductions by running a standalone 60-second ad break at the start or the end of a show. Interestingly, Pivotal Research Group Senior Analyst Brian Wieser wrote in a research note earlier this month that NBCU was far from the worst offender as far as ad load is concerned. NBCU had average ad loads of 11.1 minutes per hour in January, roughly in the middle of the 10 national U.S. TV network groups analyzed. Viacom had the highest, at 14.6 minutes per hour.

A Time and a Placed

A newsletter exclusive: You might remember Placed as the location ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 company Snap acquired for $135 million in cash last year. Now Placed, which continues to operate independently from Snap, also wants to be remembered as the go-to metric for offline location data, the company's CEO David Shim tells me. From Thursday, any brand, agency or Placed competitor will get free access to Placed's Insights dashboard (the company will also continue to operate a paid-for premium service) to get data about the types of people that visit particular stores and which other brands and categories they have an affinity with. Mr. Shim tells me that while Nielsen is seen as the standard for TV ratings and comScore for website visits, there hasn't really been a common currency for visits in the physical world. Placed gets its location data from owned-and-operated apps. Users opt-in to share their whereabouts in the background, in exchange for rewards like airline miles or charitable donations. Mr. Shim tells me it's this opt-in consent approach which puts the company in good stead when Europe's new General Data Protection Regulation comes into force in May.

Best of the rest

Pizza Hut has replaced Papa John's as the official pizza of the NFL. ["https://www.wsj.com/articles/nfl-partners-with-pizza-hut-a-day-after-split-with-papa-johns-1519822738"]

Nike defeated an appeal by a photographer who accused the company of infringing his copyright by using his iconic photo of basketball star Michael Jordan to create its "Jumpman" logo. ["https://www.reuters.com/article/us-nike-lawsuit-jordan/nike-defeats-appeal-over-iconic-michael-jordan-photo-jumpman-logo-idUSKCN1GB2R7"]

Viacom CEO Bob Bakish said the company is planning a new ad-funded over-the-top streaming service, expected to launch next year. ["http://variety.com/2018/tv/news/viacom-plots-launch-streaming-service-1202713056/"]

Vice and Vogue have put their editorial collaboration on hold just four months after it was announced. Launch sponsors including Gap had reportedly pulled support and switched their spending to other Condéast brands. ["https://digiday.com/media/vogue-vice-put-editorial-collaboration-hold/"]

Amazon is selling its first pay-per-view package for live sports, having signed a deal with UFC to stream the organization's upcoming slate of weekend fights. Aurora raises $90m as investors race into self-driving sector### invalid font number 1


Tim Bradshaw in Los Angeles

Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Aurora, a year-old start-up led by the former head of Google self-driving car unit, has raised $90m in funding, in the latest sign that investors are racing into the self-driving car market. 

The Silicon Valley company funding comes just days after California approved regulations that could see truly driverless cars appear on its roads within weeks.

Index Ventures and Greylock, the venture capital firms, jointly led the investment in Aurora. Although terms of the equity investment were not disclosed, the deal is likely to value Aurora in the hundreds of millions of dollars. 

At the same time as Aurora's funding was announced, Mr Urmson former employer, now called Waymo, sought to consolidate its early lead in autonomous technology by revealing that its vehicles had driven more than 5m miles on public roads. 

With every extra mile bringing new experiences that can teach the autonomous systems and ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 software, the pace of Waymo data gathering is also accelerating: it took six years to reach its first 1m miles but fewer than three months for the fifth million. 

oday, wee driving as many miles in one day as the average American adult drives in a whole year,Waymo said in a blog post on Wednesday. 

Mr Urmson left the Alphabet-owned company in 2016 to start Aurora with Sterling Anderson, Tesla former autopilot chief, and robotics professor Drew Bagnell, a co-founder of Uber self-driving car unit.

n a sector that has so much novelty and first-time-ness to the technology youe developing, the experience really matters,aid Mike Volpi, general partner at Index Ventures, who will be joining Aurora's board. t clear today that the leader in terms of technology is Waymo. The people who built it are here at Aurora. I pretty sure they can do it better a second time.

Reid Hoffman, co-founder of LinkedIn and a Greylock partner who will also join Aurora's board, added: ased on background alone, Chris could be considered as the Henry Ford of autonomous vehicles.

Unlike some Silicon Valley start-ups such as Zoox, which are planning to develop their own self-driving vehicle, Aurora wants to partner with traditional carmakers.

It has already struck partnerships with Volkswagen and Hyundai, as well as Chinese electric vehicle start-up Byton. The new funds will help it to accelerate development of its technology for these customers and expand its team. 

Mr Volpi said the company model to work alongside, rather than in competition with, existing carmakers was a key differentiator from rivals at a time when it was unclear whether Silicon Valley was friend or foe to the car industry.

Aurora is hoping to win customers among both manufacturers and fleet operators such as Uber or Lyft, which plan to run obo-taxiervices. 

he business models of the ncumbentsamong Silicon Valley autonomous driving companies] are a little unclear,r Volpi said. he landscape is odd and it creating a very interesting opportunity for Aurora.

Phantom Cyber Fetches $350 Million in Acquisition by Splunk; The deal marks a notable cybersecurity exit for investors including Kleiner Perkins.### invalid font number 1


By Cat Zakrzewski 
400 words
28 February 2018
00:16
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright ©018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Splunk Inc. has agreed to acquire venture-backed Phantom Cyber Corp. for about $350 million in cash and stock, as the ### invalid font number 2
big-data### invalid font number 1
 specialist seeks to expand its cybersecurity offerings.

The acquisition, Splunk's largest acquisition to date, is also a notable exit for Phantom's investors, which include Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, TechOperators Venture Capital, Blackstone, Foundation Capital, In-Q-Tel and Rein Capital. Phantom, founded four years ago, had raised about $23 million, and its valuation was just under $100 million following its funding round in January 2017, according to PitchBook Data Inc.

Tuesday's deal comes after a two-year uptick in cybersecurity M&A, with financial and strategic buyers completing $41 billion such deals, according to data tracker Momentum Cyber. Last year, another automation cybersecurity startup, Hexadite, was acquired by Microsoft Corp. for an undisclosed sum.

Phantom Cyber provides security automation and orchestration tools that help clients ensure that the existing cybersecurity products they own are working well together. By automating menial tasks, Phantom aims to ensure cybersecurity personnel have more time to focus on bigger threats.

The automation and orchestration category has been an increasingly attractive opportunity for venture investors. Companies have been spending record amounts on cybersecurity to secure their digital assets in the wake of high-profile data breaches at Equifax and Target.

But the supply of talented cybersecurity professionals hasn't kept up with the demand for their skills. Venture investors have been funding companies that can help alleviate that shortage.

Splunk Senior Vice Preisdent of Security Markets Haiyan Song said the product will bring relief to Spunk customers who have struggled to hire security teams.

"Even if they have the budget they might not be able to find the people who have the skills," Ms. Song said.

Phantom Cyber, based in Palo Alto, Calif., has about 100 employees. Chief Executive and founder Oliver Friedrichs will join Splunk, and the companies are still completing hiring plans for other Phantom employees.

Mr. Friedrichs said the Splunk acquisition will allow Phantom to consider expansion into automating and orchestrating other IT tasks beyond cybersecurity.

"Splunk is collecting, storing and analyzing data," Mr. Friedrichs said. "Phantom is all about taking action on data."

Nomad Health Inc., an online marketplace to connect health-care professionals with freelance work, has raised a $12 million Series B round.

Polaris Partners led the round, with participation from First Round Capital, RRE Ventures and .406 Ventures. The latest financing brings Nomad's total funding to date to $17 million.

Alexi Nazem, a physician, founded Nomad in 2015 with MongoDB Inc. founder Kevin Ryan seeking to remove the middleman in job-finder services. The platform allows employers and prospects to contact one another directly. The company says its platform can help organizations save up to 40% on recruitment fees.

The impetus for the company's creation was the complex and time-consuming hiring process for freelance health-care professionals. Although the health-care industry is now the largest source of jobs in the U.S., the country still grapples with a shortage of doctors and nurses. Dr. Nazem thinks Nomad could be a valuable tool in quickly filling jobs.

"For the first time ever, health care became the largest share of jobs created in the U.S. economy, and that will put more pressure on recruiters to do their job as quickly and effectively as possible," said Dr. Nazem.

Nomad is available in 12 states including California, Texas and New York. Dr. Nazem said there are over 35,000 clinical professionals on the platform and around 1,500 medical facilities.

With the new funding, the company plans growth of its sales team and will explore ways to add more capabilities to the platform. Dr. Nazem said existing customers have been asking for tools for things like onboarding and employee ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
, and he said he would eventually like Nomad to function as a workforce management platform.

Polaris Managing Partner Brian Chee will join New York-based Nomad's board of directors.

How SoftBank, World's Biggest Tech Investor, Throws Around Its Cash; SoftBank is on a shopping spree, investing billions in firms such as Uber and WeWork, both directly and through an affiliated tech fund### invalid font number 1


By Phred Dvorak and Mayumi Negishi 
2,114 words
26 February 2018
22:10
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright ©018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Corrections & Amplifications

David Chao is general partner of DCM Ventures. Plenty is a South San Francisco startup. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated Mr. Chao was a managing partner of DCM Partners and Plenty was a San Francisco startup. (Feb. 26, 2018)

Tech magnate Masayoshi Son often befuddles people in his industry with the large sums he is willing to pay for stakes in companies.

That includes his directors.

Shigenobu Nagamori says he objected when Mr. Son, chief executive of SoftBank Group Corp., told his board in 2016 he wanted to pay "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-agrees-to-buy-arm-holdings-for-more-than-32-billion-1468808434". The U.K. chip-design firm was worth a 10th of that, Mr. Nagamori, then a SoftBank outside director, says he told Mr. Son.

Mr. Son paid it anyway. And he continued a buying spree, picking up stakes in dozens more companies, many of them "http://www.wsj.com/graphics/tech-startup-stocks-to-watch/#"uch as Uber Technologies Inc. and WeWork Cos. In some of those deals, too, he had to argue with directors and advisers who thought he was paying too much.

Investors want to understand just how Mr. Son goes about deciding on his billions of dollars of bets, among the largest in the tech industry, which he does through SoftBank and "https://www.wsj.com/articles/behind-the-long-painful-birth-of-the-worlds-biggest-tech-fund-1495214782". Current and former SoftBank directors, executives, investing partners and others who know Mr. Son give a glimpse into how he works. It appears sometimes methodical, sometimes haphazard.

They describe a man who sometimes makes gut-instinct decisions in businesses he knows little aboutuch as the time he spent about 30 minutes deciding he wanted to invest $200 million in a"https://www.wsj.com/articles/indoor-farming-takes-root-at-california-startup-1486981806". Other times, he compiles an elaborate analysis, inundating his directors with hundreds of pages of documents to help explain an investment target.

To strike quickly, he sometimes commits to investments before getting approval from his fund's investment committee, some of these people say. And he often spars with his executives and board members over his proposals until they are convinced or acquiesce.

"I've opposed almost all of Mr. Son's proposed investments," says SoftBank director Tadashi Yanai, president of Fast Retailing Co., "https://www.wsj.com/articles/uniqlo-founder-thinks-u-s-needs-fewer-malls-and-more-of-his-stores-1509969603". Instead of acting like a speculative investor, he says, Mr. Son should focus on "real business." Mr. Yanai has publicly said his role is to tell Mr. Son things that are painful to hear. He and Mr. Nagamori have publicly praised Mr. Son's vision and drive, saying they support him despite reservations on investments.

Understanding the 60-year-old Mr. Son matters because of his outsize impact. SoftBank, his Tokyo-based tech-investment-and-telecom firm, has led investments totaling about $145 billion in companies since 1995, according to deal tracker Dealogic, including "https://www.wsj.com/articles/no-headline-available-1394139736" U.S. mobile carrier Sprint. He wields the world's biggest technology fund, the $92 billion Vision Fund, and a $6 billion affiliate, brandishing investments that often make him a company's biggest shareholder.

Last year, he spent around $37 billion on more than 40 companies, according to Dealogic. Among them was "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-succeeds-in-tender-offer-for-large-stake-in-uber-1514483283" for a roughly 15% share.

Many in tech finance believe his investments "https://www.wsj.com/graphics/billion-dollar-club/". "We all thought the unicorn stuff was going to start slowing down," says Tim Connors, founder of the Silicon Valley venture-capital firm PivotNorth Capital. "Then along comes SoftBank and lobs another $90 billion into what many people thought was already an overheated market."

His money makes waves beyond tech. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-is-in-talks-to-buy-big-stake-in-insurer-swiss-re-1518030022" in a deal that could be valued at $10 billion or more, The Wall Street Journal reported this month.

SoftBank declined to make Mr. Son available for an interview. In November, he told the Journal SoftBank does due diligence to ensure its investments' valuations are appropriate.

"Mr. Son's activities, including the Vision Fund, are extremely prominent and flashy, and it makes people think he's taking on a huge amount of risk," says Yasuhiro Sato, CEO of Mizuho Financial Group, SoftBank's main bank. "I can guarantee that we've been monitoring the risk very carefully."

Outlook: 00 years'

Mr. Son has said his goal is to take big stakes in a coalition of companies driving technological change that will "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-group-corp-plans-to-list-telecom-cash-cow-1517986513" He told reporters this month because the Vision Fund invests in unicorns and companies that are No. 1 in their markets, he expects "extremely high growth" from them.

Arm CEO Simon Segars, a SoftBank director, says Mr. Son's investments often are in companies that collect large amounts of data on how people live, work or move. "I can look at the majority of those investments that have been made and say, look, the data angle is this."

A Japanese of Korean descent, Mr. Son attended the University of California, Berkeley, and in 1981 founded SoftBank as a software distributor. He led its investments in more than 1,300 companies, sometimes on gut instinct. He often recounts deciding to put $20 million in a fledgling Chinese e-commerce firm named Alibaba in 2000 because of the "sparkle" in CEO Jack Ma's eyes stake now valued at about $120 billion.

He survived a close call in the dot-com bust after 1999, when "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB985815778394585467" of its value. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB106633694181524900". SoftBank is now Japan's No. 7 company in revenue, according to S&P Global Market Intelligence.

Venture investor David Chao says it took Mr. Son just around half an hour in a 2017 meeting to decide to back Plenty, a South San Francisco startup that grows vegetables indoors. Mr. Son was skeptical before the meeting, says Mr. Chao, an early Plenty investor. He gave Mr. Chao and Plenty CEO Matt Barnard 15 minutes to make their case at Mr. Son's Woodside, Calif., residence.

Mr. Chao says he brought a 7-foot growing wallhe vegetables are produced on themutting off lettuce and mustard greens for Mr. Son to try. Mr. Son quizzed Mr. Barnard and listened to his plan for indoor farms in the U.S.

Soon, Mr. Son was lecturing him to think bigger, suggesting the company expand abroad. "The next thing you know, it's like: ow much do you need and let's get this deal done,' " says Mr. Chao, general partner of DCM Ventures, in which SoftBank has a minority investment. "He doesn't let capital be a constraint."

Four months later, Mr. Son invested around $200 millionwice what Plenty had asked for, Mr. Chao said.

A Plenty spokesman declined to make Mr. Barnard available for comment.

Mr. Son has publicly praised Plenty for using ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 and "Internet of Things" technologyeaturing networks of sensors and computing chips.

Mr. Son's willingness to make quick bets surprised CEO Eugene Izhikevich of San Diego ### invalid font number 2
artificial-intelligence### invalid font number 1
 company Brain, which is developing self-driving technology. In May, Mr. Son asked how much it would take to speed Brain's rollout, Mr. Izhikevich says. Within two months, SoftBank had invested $114 million through the Vision Fund. "Something I thought would take 10 to 15 years," he says, "now I can achieve three to five times faster."

Often, Mr. Son will rough out deal terms on his own, leaving little for SoftBank and Vision Fund deal teams to do, people familiar with the investments say.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/behind-the-long-painful-birth-of-the-worlds-biggest-tech-fund-1495214782", with funds from SoftBank and investors such as Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund. The fund has its own investment committee, including Mr. Son, that approves deals. Some of its biggest deals are also brought before SoftBank's board.

The line between SoftBank and the Vision Fund isn't always clear. SoftBank units advise and manage the fund. SoftBank must offer deals of $100 million or more to the Vision Fund.

To move fast, Mr. Son sometimes agrees to deals first and passes them to the investment committee for approval later, say people familiar with the process. SoftBank's Uber investment, for instance, hasn't yet been approved by the Vision Fund's committee, although SoftBank has said it plans to transfer it to the fund.

Board persuasion

Other times, Mr. Son researches his targets extensively, spelling out scenarios for how the investment will play out. When he pitches deals to his SoftBank board, says Mr. Nagamori, the former director, he often gives it hundreds of pages of documents in advance.

Mr. Son can go "on and on" explaining his conclusions if directors challenge him, says Mr. Nagamori, CEO of precision-motor maker Nidec Corp., who says he resigned in September because it consumed too much time. "If you want to lodge a proper challenge" to Mr. Son, he says, "you need to really study upt takes a tremendous amount of time."

In some deals, SoftBank executives and directors have told Mr. Son they thought he was overpaying, say people with knowledge of those conversations.

In 2015, Mr. Son wanted to buy Arm, which designs chips for products ranging from smartphones to networked cars. Several SoftBank executives told him SoftBank didn't have the financial strength to buy Arm, persuading him to raise cash by selling some holdings first, people familiar with the discussions say. "It was like pulling teeth," one says.

By mid-2016, Mr. Son had raised the money and tracked down Arm's chairman on a yacht in the Mediterranean to propose the deal, says Mr. Segars, Arm's CEO, who was present.

Mr. Son's projections to support his $32 billion bid included a prediction that Arm-designed networked chips would dominate the market, which Mr. Nagamori says he considered too optimistic. "I said, ome competitor is bound to emerge, so you can't pay such a high price.' " He eventually relented, although he says he remains unpersuaded about the valuation.

"From the outside, some of the moves are riskier moves than most companies would take," says Mr. Segars. "But history has shown that he's been right more often than he's been wrong."

At a 2017 shareholder meeting, Mr. Son said it was natural for directors to have different opinions and dissent was a sign of healthy management.

Price concerns also emerged during Mr. Son's "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wework-a-20-billion-startup-fueled-by-silicon-valley-pixie-dust-1508424483", was pitched to SoftBank executives in early 2015. It was valued at around $5 billion then, estimates Dow Jones VentureSource, which tracks venture-capital data.

SoftBank's investment head at the time dismissed the pitch because he didn't see WeWork as a tech company and worried it was too expensive, especially given SoftBank's weak finances at the time, say people familiar with the discussion.

In January 2016, with WeWork's valuation at $10 billion, Mr. Son met CEO Adam Neumann and was intrigued by his vision of how work would evolve, says WeWork Japan CEO Chris Hill. Mr. Son told Mr. Neumann he was moving too slowly, and he responded that WeWork needed "fuel," Mr. Hill says. WeWork declined to make Mr. Neumann available for comment.

A year later, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-mulls-investment-of-over-1-billion-in-wework-1485814702", the Journal reported. Most SoftBank directors were opposed, telling him they didn't understand why SoftBank should invest in what they viewed as essentially a real-estate company, say people familiar with the discussions. Some SoftBank executives thought WeWork's valuation, then $17 billion, was excessive, says one.

Mr. Son prevailed, leading SoftBank and the Vision Fund to invest $4.4 billion in WeWork in 2017, boosting its valuation to around $20 billion. "Mr. Son has his own ideas," says a SoftBank executive who says he sees his job as telling the CEO his qualms while also supporting him.

Mr. Son's buying spree continues. In January, the Vision Fund put $300 million in Los Angeles-based dog-walking app Wag Labs Inc. During a press conference, he called it "Uber for dogs."

It also invested ¬60 million ($562.7 million) in German online used-car dealer Auto1 Group. Mr. Son urged it to take more money than it had planned and "think bigger," says Matt Krna, managing partner of tech-investment fund Princeville Global, which introduced Auto1 to SoftBank a few months ago. Auto1 declined to comment.

"Anyone can be optimistic," says Mr. Krna. "Masa really makes enormous bets on his beliefs."

### invalid font number 2
Data Matters: Why Firms Buy Media Companies; Investors' appetite for turning information into profit is driving ever-bigger deals in the publishing sector### invalid font number 1


By Joice Alves 
1,280 words
26 February 2018
13:00
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright ©018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Media and publishing companies have once again become a darling of buyout firms. In the first seven weeks of this year firms have already spent nearly $18 billion in media acquisitions, putting 2018 on track to become the largest amount invested in a single year since the global financial crisis.

In the media space, buyout houses are showing growing appetite for companies that produce data and ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 valuable to the financial service industry.

Before the global financial crisis, in 2006 and 2007, buyout firms spent nearly $100 billion in the two years combined on media companies globally but investments in the sector decreased fast, according to data provider Dealogic Ltd.

Amid declining advertising and subscriptions, firms pulled their money out of the sector, and over the past decade buyout houses have spent no more than $20 billion in a single year, Dealogic said. The figures also include film and broadcasting deals.

The $17 billion acquisition of a 55% stake in Thomson Reuters Corp.'s data and terminal by a consortium led by Blackstone Group LP comprised a majority of the cash invested by private-equity firms in media companies this year.

The Canada Pension Plan Investment Board and Singaporean sovereign-wealth fund GIC Pte. Ltd. are also part of the consortium.

Thomson Reuters is controlled by the Canadian family Thomson and has a market value of over $28 billion, according to the New York Stock Exchange.

The deal represented one of the largest buyouts in Blackstone's history, proving the strong interest the asset class has in data companies. Blackstone is one of the largest private-equity firms in the world with $387 billion of assets under management.

"Data distribution and ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 is a very fast-growing space," Martin Brand, a senior managing director at Blackstone, who worked on the Thomson Reuters deal, said in an interview to The Wall Street Journal this month.

Other recent deals in the sector include Hong-Kong based Citic Capital Partners' acquisition of Euromoney Institutional Investor PLC's Global Markets Intelligence Division in February. Citic joined with Chinese news and data provider Caixin Global to do the deal and committed to pay $180.5 million for the division, which generated £1.9 million for the financial year ending in September 2017.

The Euromoney carve-out is based in Hong Kong and provides industry, economic and corporate intelligence on emerging markets. Citic said it plans to expand the business in China, Private Equity News reported.

Economics of Data

Firms are taking a closer look at b-to-b companies that provide intelligence and data because of strong global demand for selective and analytical data. The world-wide data and business ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 market is predicted to hit over $210 billion in revenue in 2020, of which $70 billion will be spent on reporting, analysis and data warehouse tools, according to a 2017 report by U.S. advisory firm International Data Corporation. IDC expects the banking sector to spend more than all other industries on data.

As demand for business information rises, so do prices. B-to-b data and information companies can also create scale by disseminating information across multiple platforms, said Eric Lawson-Smith, a partner at Arma Partners, M&A advisory firm focused on communications, media and technology deals.

"The concept behind data is: produce it once and sell it many times," he said.

Alongside offering strong growth margins, data and intelligence companies are easy to sell and easy to buy, because banks have appetite to back leverage buyouts involving those companies, said Mr. Lawson-Smith.

From a banker's point of view, it is very easy to predict the company's performance, Mr. Lawson-Smith added. This is because a typical subscription for data services is prepaid on an annual base.

For instance, in the deal to acquire a 55% stake in Thomson Reuters' financial and risk unit, the consortium led by Blackstone will raise debt, which covers a vast majority of the $17 billion the buyers committed to pay, the Journal reported.

Thomson Reuters' news service will be split from its financial and risk business following the deal. The Thomson Reuters' financial and risk solutions spinout recently backed by Blackstone has signed a 30-year contract to use the news services, in exchange for at least $325 million a year, the Journal reported.

Arma has said that private-equity firms have been able to net multiples in the midteens from selling b-to-b information and financial data companies over the past five years.

Strong Returns

Good growth margins and easy financing means it could be the right time to sell. A string of private-equity firms have netted strong returns by exiting or selling parts of information service businesses to fellow firms or trade buyers seeking to increase their data portfolio.

"There are really exciting exit opportunities because there is a lot of interest from trade buyers," said Nathaniel Cooper, executive director media and technology at advisory firm DC Partners.

For example, last year Sweden-based private-equity firm EQT sold data provider Bureau Van Dijk to Moody's Corp. for ¬ billion, nearly twice the amount it paid to buy the company in July 2014, Moody's said.

Amsterdam-based Bureau, which was founded in 1991, had four private-equity owners between 2004 and 2017.

EQT bought the company in 2014 from Charterhouse Capital Partners, which had acquired it in 2011 from BC Partners. Bureau's first private-equity owner was Candover Investments, which sold the business to BC for £00 million in 2007.

The company provides information on more than 220 million companies and helps financial institutions, professional services firms and governments with credit analysis, investment research, tax analysis, compliance and due diligence.

In 2016, Bureau had revenue of $281 million and earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortization of $144 million, Moody's said last year announcing the deal.

London-based buyout firm BC Partners bought Mergermarket for £82 million in 2014 when it carved the business out from publisher Pearson PLC and sold a minority stake in the business to Singapore's sovereign-wealth fund GIC last July valuing the company at £ billion. Under the firm's ownership the company has seen its Ebitda double, Private Equity News previously reported.

Mergermarket, which was founded in 2000 and rebranded Acuris after the deal with GIC, provides financial data and analysis. The company employs more than 1,000 staff in 65 locations across the world.

Creating Profit

Given the promising estimations for the sector, private-equity firms are making an effort to tap into media and information businesses.

Blackstone has hired data scientists to help it evaluate how to create profit for its new data business Thomson Reuters' financial and risk unit, the Journal reported.

The companies achieving the highest returns on their initial investment are those that can combine data, intelligence and software that simplifies and selects data, Mr. Lawson-Smith said.

U.S. technology company IBM Corp. estimated in a 2017 report that every day, the world's population creates 2.5 quintillion bytes of data. About two decades ago, the value of b-to-b information companies was measured based on the quantity of data they were able to deliver, but now there is almost too much of it for institutions to use effectively, Mr. Lawson-Smith said.

"I think today in the data market, the most attractive businesses are businesses that can provide not just data but genuine, insightful analyses into that data," he said.

China's Authoritarian State Has an Edge in Artificial Intelligence Development; A wealthy repressive state can press ahead with tech innovation unimpeded by privacy concerns### invalid font number 1

China made enormous economic strides in the past quarter-century by manufacturing everything from toys to tires inexpensively and exporting them overseas. To become a truly wealthy nation, it must move beyond its role as a low-cost manufacturer and become an economic innovator itself.

Can a repressive state, led by a central government specializing in five-year plans and surveillance of its own people, make such a leap? The odds against success look steep. Economic history includes few examples of authoritarian states becoming innovative business leaders. But China aims to make that jump "https://www.wsj.com/articles/why-u-s-companies-may-lose-the-ai-race-1516280677"r high-level ### invalid font number 2
machine learning### invalid font number 1
ith an unusual approach that can't be dismissed.

Beijing is bankrolling "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-and-intel-beware-china-is-gunning-for-dominance-in-ai-chips-1515060224", in part, to keep better track of homegrown individuals it considers criminals and dissidents, and to intimidate would-be opponents. That work involves fundamental research in image recognition, data collection and sorting that could have commercial spinoffs in the software used to run complex systems. A city with millions of self-driving cars, for instance, would need data analysis and the ability to recognize, say, that a ball bouncing across the street might be followed by a child chasing it.

"What gives China an edge is there is more of a sense of urgency," says Paul Triolo, a technology research manager at Eurasia Group.

Last summer, China's central government published a comprehensive plan for ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 development that aims to make the nation "the world's primary AI innovation center" by 2030.

In China such plans aren't simply blueprints, but indications of central government priorities, which work down to localities, state-owned firms and entrepreneurs. So far, local governments, looking to turn themselves into software centers, have pledged about $7 billion in funding for AI development, estimates a congressional panel that focuses on China.

Mix that with genuine enthusiasm for the technology among Chinese scientists and companiesnd "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-searches-for-path-back-to-china-with-artificial-intelligence-lab-1513162471"nd you have a formidable force, say U.S. researchers who have studied China's AI program.

"There's an enthusiasm for AI and culture of dynamism that we don't see as much in the U.S., at least outside of Silicon Valley," says Massachusetts Institute of Technology researcher Erik Brynjolfsson.

Among the most innovativend threateningf the Chinese research involves surveillance. The Wall Street Journal has detailed how Beijing has turned its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/twelve-days-in-xinjiang-how-chinas-surveillance-state-overwhelms-daily-life-1513700355" into a warren of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-all-seeing-surveillance-state-feared-in-the-west-is-a-reality-in-china-1498493020" to track millions of Uighur minorities. Police there use hand-held devices to search smartphones for encrypted chat apps. A new twist: mobile facial-recognition units "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-police-go-robocop-with-facial-recognition-glasses-1518004353" that police use to search crowds for fugitives, among other uses.

### invalid font number 2
Artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 works by the collection of vast amounts of data used to "train" computer software to recognize patterns and reach conclusions, precisely the kind of skills needed to track a small number of dissidents among a sea of Chinese faces.

"A repressive state can be an engine of innovation," says Robert Atkinson, president of the Information Technology and Innovation Foundation, a tech policy center.

In the past, Chinese efforts to innovate have stumbled over the state's desire to centralize control, denying researchers and companies the freedom to follow their own ideas. China, for instance, has tried to jump-start its semiconductor industry with projects dating as far back as the Cultural Revolution, but still hasn't produced a company that can compete with global leaders like Intel or Samsung. Beijing is trying once again i"https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinas-next-target-u-s-microchip-hegemony-1501168303", putting together a massive government fund, but the U.S. government so far has blocked China's efforts to acquired advanced Western technology.

AI may be different, say U.S. technology researchers. China's AI effort is being joined by globally competitive internet powerhouses including Tencent Holdings Ltd., which is focused on medical imaging, and Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., which wants to help create smart cities that use sensors, cameras and computers to manage traffic. So far, the government is following their lead in technology development, AI researchers say, whatever the goals laid out in the government plan.

Chinese researchers may not have political freedom, they say, but they have the economic freedom to chart their own course. And the Chinese government doesn't throw up roadblocks to the technology on the grounds of privacy, as occurs in the West.

"Freedom is very important," says Mr. Brynjolfsson. "But there is more freedom in China to do startups than most people realize."

Becoming an innovator in high technology and making a business success of those innovations in international markets is something that only a few countries have done, says Loren Graham, an emeritus MIT science historian.

"All of themo farre open, democratic societies in which government companies do not dominate," he said.

He says China's push in AI represents a great economic experiment.

"Can a country like China with lots of money combine repression, creativity and economic success based on that creativity? If the answer is yes, then we will have to rethink everything."

Low volatility funds fail to protect investors### invalid font number 1


KATE BEIOLEY

FTFT

Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

INVESTING Specialist ETFs lose out during recent market correction

A number of exchange traded funds (ETFs) designed to protect investors from sharp stock market gyrations lost more money than mainstream US stocks during this month's selloff, underperforming in precisely the conditions in which they were meant to thrive.

Low volatility ETFs, launched by asset managers including BlackRock and State Street, were billed as a way of buying the least volatile parts of a market or index such as the S&P 500. They mimic the return of a main index but are weighted towards low volatility stocks and are designed to lose less in the event of a correction.

Yet, instead of protecting UK investors against the sharp sell-off in US stocks this month, the three ETFs tracking low volatility versions of the S&P 500 lost more than the mainstream index over some of the most volatile days.

The US stock market slide began on February 2, following the publication of positive US jobs data. Over the next seven days, the S&P 500 index fell 2.9 per cent, while investors in Blackrock's $1.4bn iShares Edge S&P 500 Minimum Volatility Ucits ETF lost 4.1 per cent over the period and State Street's SPDR S&P 500 was down 4.4 per cent in total return terms.

Another fund designed to give a higher yield than mainstream stocks is the Power-Shares S&P 500 High Dividend Low Volatility Ucits ETF. It lost less over the week, at just under 2 per cent. But over the month to February 15 it had also lost more than the index.

Investors in SPDR S&P 500 Ucits ETF have also experienced a more turbulent ride than the broader index over six months, according to risk data from FE ### invalid font number 2
Analytics### invalid font number 1
, with the share price fluctuating more dramatically than the mainstream benchmark.

Critics say US low volatility strategies are vulnerable to interest rate rises because they are more heavily weighted towards sectors such as utilities, which are vulnerable when rates and bond yields rise, and were punished in the recent correction.

"People walk into low volatility ETFs thinking they will deliver the same performance as the widermarket with lower volatility. But that is not the case," said Shaun Port, chief investment officer at Nutmeg, an online investment platform. "Minimum volatility ETFs are also a bet on bond yields and you have to be cautious about usingthem." Analysts said former low volatility stocks could become Higherrisk as the economic climateshifted.

"Among the potential pitfalls to low volatility INVESTING is the reliance on past risk data," said Hortense Bioy, director of passive fund research at Morningstar. "There isnoguarantee that the least volatile stocks historically will remain so going forward. In fact, these stocksmay become more volatile as more money flows into them and their valuations increase, as has been witnessed in recent years."

Low volatility ETFstend to favour bond-like stocks that have offered steady dividends in aperiod of ultra-low interest rates. However, they have become increasingly popular and expensive as money has flowed into asset managers' lowvolatility products.

Higher interest rates benefit financial stocks and other sectors that have traditionally been seen as Higherrisk; these are also the sectors to which low volatility ETFs are less exposed.

BlackRock limits the extent to which its iShares S&P 500 Minimum Volatility ETF can diverge from the S&P 500 to manage its stock and sector risk. But the ETF still looks very different from a mainstream US fund, with far less exposure to tech giants such as Amazon and Apple and greater exposure to utilities andconsumerstaples stocks.

BlackRock and State Streetinsisted their Low volatility funds fulfil their function over the long term. State Street added that low volatility ETFs perform best in periods of extended downturn but cannot be expected to work in all conditions or in short periods ofvolatility .

iShares Said thatonthe basis of a net asset value return, based on the change in the value of the fund's assets rather than share price, its low volatility ETF had outperformed the S&P 500 index during the period ofvolatility .

Anthony Kruger of iShares European specialist sales team said: "The iShares S&PMinVol ETF has performed as it was designed to during the recent market sell-offandhas outperformed the parent benchmark ona relative basis."

Beijing Tightens Reins on Business### invalid font number 1


By James T. Areddy 
921 words
24 February 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SHANGHAI -- When Anbang Insurance Group Co. paid about $2 billion to buy New York City's Waldorf Astoria Hotel three years ago, the deal seemed to define an era for China Inc. President Xi Jinping shortly afterward dropped in to stay at the Park Avenue landmark.

China's business priorities have since changed, turning real-estate trophies into symbols of risk. Regulators in Beijing on Friday said they seized control of Anbang to keep the closely held insurer from collapsing, while prosecutors in Shanghai said they indicted Wu Xiaohui, Anbang's swashbuckling ex-chairman, for alleged fraudulent fundraising and abuse of power.

China's government makes no secret of its penchant to guide commerce, even with private companies, but the boardroom takeover still rattled analysts used to Beijing's applying its influence more quietly. "This is an unprecedented step, putting into receivership a Chinese company in such a public direct way," said Scott Kennedy at the Washington-based Center for Strategic and International Studies. "They are so worried about risks that they will stop at nothing to avoid them."

The move, which some view as a quasi-nationalization of Anbang, sheds light on how far these companies have fallen out of favor with President Xi's government. Other high-profile private businesses were already signaling strategy shifts by selling assets and restructuring debt. They included real estate developer-turned-Hollywood producer Dalian Wanda Group; HNA Group Co., a conglomerate founded from an airline; and Tomorrow Group, a warehouse of financial businesses that has donated millions of dollars to institutions such as Harvard University.

Anbang declined to comment on the latest government statements, but in recent months it has said its business remained solid. Mr. Wu was first detained last June and hasn't made any comment. It isn't known if he has a lawyer.

Authorities began leaning on companies such as Anbang, Wanda and HNA -- dubbed "gray rhinos," or obvious-but-ignored risks -- in late 2016, after the central bank spent some $1 trillion in about 18 months to offset the effects of capital flight.

Insurers, some of which had turned to selling investment deals rather than traditional insurance, faced special scrutiny. One company's chairman was banned from the industry for a decade. Anbang, which will now be run by a working group led by a China Insurance Regulatory Commission official, has been guided by regulators since Mr. Wu's departure last June.

Even before regulators swooped down on Mr. Wu, Anbang was adjusting its sales practices and reporting good results. Regulatory filings also show that beginning last year it had pared its shareholdings in major Chinese banks, though according to the latest filings, it indirectly still held more than 15% in China Minsheng Bank in September and nearly 11% of China Merchants Bank Co.

Anbang now faces prospects of unwinding international holdings -- possibly the Waldorf in a sort of echo of a Tokyo developer's exit from New York's Rockefeller Center within seven years of its 1989 investment.

Chinese authorities allege companies such as Anbang got big through "regulatory arbitrage" -- taking advantage of incomplete financial oversight to raise money in gray markets -- and that a heavy hand is needed now to eliminate big threats to the broader financial system.

Pressure has been mounting for years on corporate China to manage debt, which International Monetary Fund analysts recently pegged at 135% of the underlying economy.

The new plan for Anbang aims to keep it from collapsing, according to the insurance regulator. But few analysts said they believe its potential demise would threaten China's financial system, the way regulators in the U.S. feared American International Group Inc. was "too big to fail" before rallying an $85 billion bailout in 2008. A report last August from UBS pegged the Chinese insurer's assets at 2.5 trillion yuan ($393.8 billion) at the end of 2016, or 3.4% of gross domestic product, but played down its potential systemic risks since all but 11% of that was in liquid investments such as stocks.

Beijing is allowing some debt fallout elsewhere in the economy, such as by permitting an increasing number of corporate-bond defaults and even publicly admonishing provincial governments for doctoring economic growth statistics.

"The case could be a signal for other investors that regulators are tightening," says Tiecheng Yang, a financial regulatory lawyer at Han Kun Law Offices in Beijing. Next, Mr. Yang says, authorities could restrict deal making between bank, securities and insurance firms that share an owner. Last month, China's banking regulator outlined new shareholder limits, including requiring permission for purchases of 5% or more in a commercial bank.

At its peak, Wanda, controlled by billionaire Wang Jianlin, had more than a half-dozen real-estate projects in development across the globe. Its overseas portfolio included the AMC movie-theater chain, Hollywood producer Legendary Entertainment, British yacht maker Sunseeker and the Ironman Triathlon.

But since mid-2017, Wanda has been in retreat, selling property projects in the U.K. and Australia, plus its Chinese theme parks, movie theaters and film sound stages.

Not long ago, Anbang's hotel purchases, Wanda's forays into Hollywood and HNA's logistics bids around the world appeared to fit Beijing's desire to own brands. Now, China's political priorities have shifted toward technology such as ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 and electric vehicles.

Tech start-ups strengthen EU ties amid uncertainty over hiring### invalid font number 1


ALIYA RAM

22 February 2018

Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Contingency plans

Prolonged uncertainty over the UK's post-Brexit policies on immigration and passporting rights has pushed many technology entrepreneurs and start-ups to strengthen their businesses in mainland Europe.

Currencycloud, the payments company backed by Google Ventures, is one of a number of tech companies to have begun implementing Brexit contingency plans, establishing offices or applying for regulatory licences outside the UK because of concerns about hiring staff and accessing clients after Brexit.

"While we'd love for the UK to have a soft Brexit, you can't guarantee that," said Todd Latham, chief marketing officer and head of product at Currencycloud, which has applied for a regulatory licence in the Netherlands. "Our concern is about how we service customers and ensure that we're bringing the best talent to the organisation." Start-ups are seen as a crucial part of the tech "ecosystem" because of their role in encouraging innovation. The government has attempted to reassure the £70bn tech industry that Brexit will not affect its ability to hire highly skilled coders or do business in the EU.

Over the past year, Theresa May, prime minister, and Philip Hammond, chancellor, have unveiled initiatives, including plans to double the number of Tier 1 visas for "exceptional talent" and set up an office to promote start-ups in ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 research.

However, smaller start-ups without resources to support visa applications complain they will face a severe shortage if they cannot rely on hiring developers from the EU. A fifth of tech jobs in London are filled by workers from the bloc, said the Recruitment & Employment Confederation. Across the UK, they hold 7 per cent of all tech roles, the Department for Exiting the EU said.

Company founders claim hundreds of developers have rejected jobs in the UK since the referendum due to uncertainty over future status. "The number of candidates we've had applying from Spain or France has completely dropped off," said Aneesh Varma, founder of a start-up, Aire, that creates credit scores.

The UK has historically served as Europe's main start-up hub because of London's popularity as a base for venture capital investors. But since the EU vote, countries such as France, the Netherlands, Germany and Spain have begun to encroach on it, announcing more fast-track visas and holding events to lure high-profile investors.

A quarter of UK start-up founders surveyed by Silicon Valley Bank this year said they would open a European outpost because of Brexit. The founders and chiefs of a number of other startups ncluding Ensygnia, Inploi, Cablato, Kantox, Ecoisme, Avespa, ETFmatic and Aire lso confirmed they were strengthening non-UK operations.

"The market in the UK feels quite sour and it's going to have an impact on our ability to hire," said Matthew de la Hey, co-founder of Inploi, a platform for connecting employers with hospitality workers, which is raising capital for an office in the EU this year.

"We have found it more difficult to do business in the UK [since the vote]." Markets page 25

### invalid font number 2
Someone is wrong on the internet, consumer financial regulation edition### invalid font number 1


Matthew C Klein 
924 words
21 February 2018
06:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

It been just over a decade since the Great Recession officially began. Apparently that long enough for people who should know better to pretend nothing ever happened.

Todd Zywicki, a law professor at George Mason University, says financial products aimed at American consumers should be regulated to prevent nfair and deceptive actions His suggested reforms of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, however, do not appear to be informed by the experience of the past 15 years.

A quick refresher:

Most people can buy housing without borrowing large sums. This means small changes in credit supply inimum down payment requirements, maximum debt-to-income ratios, minimum credit scores, etc an have large effects on how much money can be spent on housing. Looser credit boosts the share of people who own rather than rent, but the bigger impact is on house prices, construction activity, and total indebtedness.

For a variety of reasons, underwriting standards collapsed in the mid-2000s. There are many ways to demonstrate this, but perhaps the most obvious is the changing way additional mortgage debt was funded. From the early 1990s through the early 2000s, the share of net new mortgage debt funded by the so-called rivate-labelond market hovered around 10 per cent. In the mid-2000s this share ballooned to more than half:

This market then disappeared after a wave of defaults. Essentially all of the net decline in mortgage debt since the peak in 2009 can be explained by the collapse of these products. More traditional forms of mortgage debt held up relatively well.

The resulting credit boom and bust was a disaster for everyone. The borrowers who temporarily enefitedrom this were disproportionately poorer and less-educated. They often found themselves burdened with products that gave them ccesso credit at the cost of usurious fees. When the cycle turned they lost everything and generally ended up worse than they were before.

(Even if these borrowers hadn ended up worse than before the bubble, psychologists have found that temporary gains followed by large losses are more painful than a steady state.)

Given all this, it not surprising that legislators decided to create a new regulatory agency meant to protect borrowers and savers from certain products. If there is a case for regulating medicines, food, and motor vehicles, it makes sense to also regulate financial products that can have enormous consequences for our net worth, income security, and overall well-being.

Yet here is Zywicki first priority:

Regulations that eliminate credit options for those who already have few of them will not make their lives better. Working with the private sector and Congress to reverse this growing exclusion of Americans from the financial sector is a moral imperative.

This is code for loosening up on payday lenders and others who prey on the well-known inability of many people to understand the implications of compound interest.

Zywicki also wants to return to the bad old days of ontraditional credit scoringhich he thinks could be made to work with ### invalid font number 2
Big Data### invalid font number 1
 His professed goal is to ffer hope for those who don have access to mainstream banking.here are often good reasons why standards exist the way they are. The last time lenders experimented with ontraditionaleasures to increase ccesshey encouraged widespread fraud and excess credit growth that was bad for everyone.

Zywicki also cares about hoicend espect claiming that merican families know better than bureaucrats what financial products best meet their needs The most pernicious of the pre-crisis financial products were notoriously difficult to understand. This was by design eople won mind being ripped off if they can see it happening.

Choice is valuable, but only if paired with transparency and clear punishments for fraud. Unfortunately, neither of those seem to be among Zywicki priorities. Similarly, Zwyicki does not seem to appreciate the difference between isrespectnd worrying about how opacity affects the functioning of a market for complex services.

Intriguingly, Zywicki concludes his column by claiming that financial institutions need more regulatory protection from borrowers:

Protecting consumers from unfair and deceptive actions by financial institutions is the CFPB core mission. But not every consumer who fails to pay his bills is a hapless victim. Smarter regulation would recognize this. During the financial crisis, for example, millions of borrowers walked away from mortgages they could afford to pay simply because they were underwater. But the CFPB mortgage rules are designed only to ensure that borrowers have the ability to pay; they do not consider precautions such as requiring larger down payments that would address the incentive to default strategically when housing prices fall.

It unclear from the column why sophisticated profit-seeking lenders should need government bureaucrats to tell them the optimal level of down payments. Nonspecialists struggling to make ends meet are supposed to be left to their own meagre devices under the principles of hoicend espect Why should the banks be any different?

Prolonged uncertainty over the UK post-Brexit policies on immigration and passporting rights has pushed many British tech entrepreneurs and start-ups to strengthen their businesses in mainland Europe.

Currencycloud, the payments company backed by Google Ventures, is one of a number of technology companies that have begun implementing their Brexit contingency plans, establishing offices or applying for regulatory licenses outside the UK because of concerns about hiring staff and accessing clients after Britain leaves the EU.

hile we love for the UK to have a soft Brexit, you can guarantee that,aid Todd Latham, chief marketing officer and head of product at Currencycloud, which has applied for a regulatory license in the Netherlands. ur concern is about how we service customers and how we make sure that wee bringing the best talent into the organisation.

Start-ups are seen as a crucial part of the tech cosystemecause of the role they play in encouraging innovation. The UK government has attempted to reassure the country £70bn tech industry that Brexit will not affect its ability to hire highly skilled coders or do business in the EU.

Over the past year, Prime Minister Theresa May and chancellor Philip Hammond have unveiled a number of initiatives, including plans to double the number of ier 1isas for xceptional talentnd establish a new office in government to promote start-ups in ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 research.

However, smaller start-ups without the resources to support visa applications complain they will face a severe talent shortage if they cannot rely on hiring developers from the EU. A fifth of tech jobs in London are filled by workers from the bloc, according to data from the Recruitment and Employment Confederation. Across the country as a whole, they hold 7 per cent of all tech roles, according to an analysis by the department for exiting the EU.

Company founders say hundreds of developers have rejected jobs in the UK since the referendum because of uncertainty over their future status. he number of candidates wee had applying from Spain or France has completely dropped off,aid Aneesh Varma, founder of Aire, a start-up that creates credit scores.

The UK has historically served as Europe main start-up centre because of London popularity as a base for venture capital investors. But since the EU referendum, countries including France, the Netherlands, Germany and Spain have begun to encroach on its dominance, announcing more fast-track visas and holding events to attract high-profile investors.

A quarter of UK start-up founders surveyed by Silicon Valley Bank this year said they would open a European outpost because of Brexit. The founders and chief executives of a number of other start-ups ncluding Ensygnia, Inploi, Cablato, Kantox, Ecoisme, Avespa, ETFmatic and Aire lso confirmed they were strengthening non-UK operations before a final deal is struck.

he market in the UK feels quite sour and it going to have an impact on our ability to hire people,aid Matthew de la Hey, co-founder of Inploi, a platform for connecting employers with hospitality workers, which is currently raising capital to establish an office in the EU this year. e have found it much more difficult to do business in the UK [since the vote].

Many tech companies are shelving plans to hire staff in London, are opening offices in other European countries and applying to EU regulators for the right to sell financial services in the bloc in case the UK does not secure passporting rights, which enable fintech and other companies to offer financial services across the EU.

In a sign of the pace of change, Germany now has 3 per cent more skilled computer developers than the UK; last year it had 5 per cent fewer, according to data from Atomico, the venture capital group. French start-ups meanwhile agreed more funding deals than the UK for the first time in five years last year.

ost of the jurisdictions wee been in contact with have set up special processes to lure away UK companies,aid Luis Rivera, chief executive of ETFmatic, a fintech start-up that has applied for a licence in Madrid. ee not exactly sure how Brexit will end up but in the worst Brexit scenariose will have to evaluate whether it is worth even being regulated in the UK at all.

WASHINGTONales of previously owned U.S. homes in January experienced their sharpest annual drop in more than three years as low inventories and rising prices and interest rates took a toll.

"https://www.nar.realtor/newsroom/existing-home-sales-slip-32-percent-in-january" 3.2% in January from the previous month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 5.38 million, the National Association of Realtors said Wednesday. Economists had expected sales to improve modestly last month after sales also declined in December.

Compared with a year earlier, sales in January were down 4.8%, the steepest annual decline since August 2014.

"Folks are looking at these ... hot markets. Prices are going up, there are fierce battles and all these homes are going for more than the asking price," said Alex Villacorta, executive vice president of HouseCanary, a housing data and ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 startup. "If you have a home at a really good mortgage rate, you're less inclined to give that up to get into a market that feels like 2004."

The drop was surprising given that pending home sales increased slightly over the past two months, which typically predicts an uptick in existing home sales. David Berson, chief economist at Nationwide Insurance, said this might partly reflect buyers who experienced "rate shock" when mortgage rates rose and they were unable to close.

The average rate nationwide for a 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage climbed roughly a quarter of a percentage point to 4.22% by the beginning of February from 3.95% at the beginning of January, according to mortgage-finance giant Freddie Mac. Rates on Thursday stood at 4.38%, according to Freddie Mac. Borrowing costs still remain low by historical standards.

"I still think we will see the job market and demographics overcome the rise in mortgage rates and give some upward movement in home sales, but much less than in the past because the number of homes for sale is so small," Mr. Berson said.

The tax-code overhaul passed in December limited deductibility of very large mortgages as well as state and local taxes, including property-tax bills. Mr. Yun said tax reform doesn't appear to be denting home sales yet, as buying activity remains strong in expensive regions and prospective buyers expressed limited concern to their Realtors, according to NAR.

A shortage of available houses on the market has driven up prices in many regions. There was a 3.4 month-supply of homes on the market at the end of January, based on the current sales pace. Total housing inventory declined in January from a year earlier to 1.52 million existing homes for sale, the lowest January count since 1999 when the agency began tracking the data.

Earlier in the year, strong home sales seemed to be "defying gravity" given low levels of inventory. "That's clearly been exhausted now," said Aaron Terrazas, senior economist at home-listings website Zillow. News Corp, owner of The Wall Street Journal, also operates Realtor.com under license from the National Association of Realtors.

Several factors are converging to buttress demand. The national unemployment rate in January was 4.1%, holding at a 17-year low. Gauges of household confidence remain elevated.

But limited inventory on the market is driving up home prices at a rapid pace, potentially blocking some would-be buyers. The median sale price for an existing home in January was $240,500, up 5.8% from a year earlier.

In several of the largest metropolitan areas, a household making a typical salary would have to spend more than 30% of their income level that economists consider financially sustainableo afford the median home payment, according to HouseCanary.

In some of the fastest-growing and priciest metropolitan areas, affordability barriers are much steeper. A typical buyer in San Francisco, for example, would need to spend 75% of household income to afford a home.
