Gormley's Take: Startups See Opportunity in Medicare Advantage's Boom; Private alternative to original Medicare covers a third of people with Medicare### invalid font number 1


By Brian Gormley 
343 words
16 February 2018
12:30
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright ©018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Medicare Advantage's explosive growth is creating opportunity for entrepreneurs.

In Medicare Advantage, an alternative to original Medicare, the government pays private insurers to cover Medicare benefits. Over the last decade Medicare Advantage enrollment has more than doubled to 19 million, and one-third of people with Medicare are now enrolled in these plans, according to the Kaiser Family Foundation.

That percentage will rise to 41% of Medicare beneficiaries in 2026, according to the foundation.

Medicare Advantage plans practice the value-based care model that is widely discussed but only gradually making its way into non-Medicare insurance. Medicare Advantage plans have incentive to lower costs because they keep the difference between what they spend on care and the amount they are paid. But they have to lower costs while still delivering quality care, which is reflected to the public through a star rating system.

Medicare Advantage plans can cover things that original Medicare doesn't, such as routine dental care. And to baby boomers, they generally feel more like the HMO they had with their employer, said Darren Schulte, chief executive of Apixio Inc., a health-care data-science startup serving Medicare Advantage plans.

Apixio, which began 2017 with 23 customers and now has 36, is one company benefiting from the program's rise. The San Mateo, Calif., company's ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
-based system helps these plans better represent the health status of their members so they can be reimbursed appropriately.

Startups that help health plans avoid unnecessary medical procedures can also profit from Medicare Advantage's growth, says David Friend, chief transformation officer of the BDO Center for Healthcare Excellence & Innovation. This could include technologies that predict health crises before they occur and tools that improve adherence to medication.

Medicare Advantage is "on the forefront of paying for value," Dr. Schulte says.

Congress Lacks Will to Overhaul Fannie and Freddie

A bipartisan group of Senate moderates works feverishly for months to craft a complex overhaul of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. The lawmakers prod progressive Democrats and right-wing Republicans, but prove unable to generate support beyond a group of centrists. The bill clears a key Senate panel but ultimately faces obstacles from leadership more focused on the midterm elections.

These dynamics were in play the last time a group of Senate centrists sought to revamp Fannie and Freddie in 2014. The same scenes are being repeated this year"https://www.wsj.com/articles/fix-for-mortgage-giants-fannie-mae-freddie-mac-remains-on-back-burner-1505508211" to overhaul the mortgage-finance giants, which have been under government control for nearly 10 years.

"The effort has been met with resistance from both ends of the ideological spectrum and neither base has moved meaningfully toward the center given the combination of complex issues and a lack of legislative urgency," said Isaac Boltansky of Compass Point Research & Trading.

To be sure, some current and former congressional aides "https://www.wsj.com/articles/congress-to-tackle-dodd-frank-rollback-fannie-freddie-overhaul-1514853477", particularly because the Trump administration wants to accomplish something in this space. Notably, House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) has shifted his views and now says he is now willing to consider an explicit federal guarantee on mortgage securities significant concession.

Still, no deal is imminent in the Senate, according to aides. And unlike in 2014, the House has yet to even introduce its own version of the legislation. A plan by Mr. Hensarling that would have liquidated Fannie and Freddie had at least passed the House in 2013.

Centrist Senate lawmakers say they continue to negotiate with their more-partisan colleagues and could win the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ackermans-take-fannie-freddie-overhaul-hinges-on-democrats-1516190202" needed to advance the bill through the chamber.

Yet some liberals say that "nothing done" is the best scenario for them, according to lobbyists and aides. They know that whatever legislation is signed into law will likely define the market for the next generation, and are willing to wait until they have a chance of controlling at least one of the chambers of Congress before striking a compromise.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

SEC Rejects Sale of Chicago Stock Exchange

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-rejects-sale-of-chicago-stock-exchange-1518738335" on Thursday rejected the sale of the Chicago Stock Exchange to a group that would have included Chinese investors, capping a two-year battle over a deal that sparked political opposition in Congress.

The SEC's commissioners voted against the transaction six months after the agency's staff recommended approving the sale. The decision is a rare move by the SEC to choose who isn't allowed to control a U.S. stock exchange, even one that handles less than 1% of trading volume.

Critics of the sale, including lawmakers from both parties, said letting a Chinese company take a major stake in a U.S. stock exchange would create a backdoor for the Chinese government to influence American financial infrastructure. The Chicago company repeatedly said the Chinese government wasn't involved in the deal, but a political fight erupted that prompted two of the initial Chinese investors to withdraw from the group in October.

U.S. Bancorp Charged Over Relationship With Race-Car Driver Scott Tucker

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-bancorp-charged-over-relationship-with-race-car-driver-scott-tucker-1518710467" $613 million Thursday for what regulators and prosecutors said were shoddy anti-money-laundering controls, a rare regulatory setback for a bank that emerged from the financial crisis relatively unscathed.

The U.S. attorney's office in Manhattan also announced criminal charges against the bank that eventually could be dismissed. The Manhattan U.S. Attorney, Geoffrey Berman, said the bank's poor controls had allowed a former customer, race-car driver Scott Tucker, to launder money from an illegal payday-lending scheme. Mr. Tucker was convicted of fraud last year.

GOP Senators Pan Trump Plan to Make Futures Industry Pay User Fees

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/gop-senators-pan-trump-plan-to-make-futures-industry-pay-user-fees-1518718264" criticized the Trump administration's proposal to impose fees on the futures industry to fund the top U.S. derivatives regulator, which has requested a bigger budget.

User fees have also been opposed by the industry and the leaders of the regulator, the Commodity Futures Trading Commission, meaning the plan stands little chance of becoming reality. By floating the idea of funding the CFTC partly with user fees, the Trump administration has acknowledged the regulator's plea for more funds, but without shifting money from elsewhere in the government's budget to the CFTC.

The impasse suggests the agency could see its funding remain flat for a fifth consecutive year, though Republicans at a Senate hearing Thursday showed some willingness to consider higher funding levels for the CFTC, citing its need for funds to support more data analysis and market surveillance.

Investors Warned of Cryptocurrency ump-and-Dump' Schemes

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/investors-warned-of-cryptocurrency-pump-and-dump-schemes-1518724576" consumers to beware of "pump-and-dump" manipulation schemes in virtual-currency markets as they try to rein in misconduct on unregulated spot exchanges.

The guidance from the Commodity Futures Trading Commission targets schemes that rely on coordinated efforts to create phony demand for cryptocurrencies, followed by schemers quickly selling their stakes to take advantage of the inflated prices.

The CFTC advisory is part of a broader consumer-education effort related to virtual currencies such as bitcoin, which have seen a swell of interest from retail investors in recent months.

Global pharmaceutical company Roche has agreed to buy Flatiron Health, a start-up focused on using technology to improve cancer treatment, whose previous investors have included the venture capital arm of Google parent company Alphabet.

Roche had previously held a 12.6 per cent equity stake in Flatiron. The agreement announced on Thursday will see it acquire the remaining portion of the company, which will keep operating as a separate legal entity.

The deal is expected to close in the latter half of 2018.

Flatiron develops technology to help manage cancer treatments, as well as collecting and analysing data about the disease for researchers. Its founders, Nat Turner and Zach Weinberg, sold their previous start-up venture, Invite Media, to a Google subsidiary in 2010. They left to start Flatiron Health in 2012.

Interest rates are on the rise, but that hasn't curbed Americans' appetite for consumer debt.

If anything, consumers are borrowing more on credit cards or through auto loans than they have in years, and lenders seeking growth are happy to oblige them.

Abe Schilling, a 33-year-old car salesman in Great Falls, Mont., said he signed up for more than five credit cards over the past year, from issuers including Capital One Financial Corp. and Discover Financial Services, after he received offers in the mail. He also took out a $36,000 loan to buy a new Jeep Grand Cherokee.

Mr. Schilling, who rents his home, said the offers have been arriving as his credit score has improved. He previously had dozens of collections and other negative marks on his credit reports after failing to pay back bills. With a steady income and months of debt counseling behind him, Mr. Schilling says he feels confident in his ability to pay for his debts.

So do plenty of other Americans. In the fourth quarter, consumer debt, excluding mortgages and other home loans, rose 5.5% from a year earlier to $3.82 trillion. That is the highest amountsince the Federal Reserve Bank of New York began tracking the data in 1999. Moreover, consumers' nonhousing debts accounted for just over 29% of their overall debt load, also the highest amount on record.

Many observers say they aren't worried yet. Their concerns about nonhousing credit are nascent because delinquencies are rising from historically low levels. Also, household debt including mortgages, the biggest category, barely edged up from the third quarter and remains well off the crisis-era highs as a percentage of U.S. economic output.

"In good times, people tend to take on a little more consumer credit because they think 'Well, my income looks good, my job looks very stable,' and when the economy turns down, they dial that back," said Tom Miller, professor of finance at Mississippi State University.

Still, there are signs that consumers' ability to handle these increased debt levels is getting strained. Student loans, the largest debt category consumers hold after mortgages, continue to have persistently high delinquencies.

Overall, households are paying about 5.8% of their disposable personal income to stay current on nonmortgage debts, according to third-quarter Federal Reserve data. This figure, which is at the highest level since the end of 2008, bottomed out at 4.9% in 2012.

An improving economy has fueled much of the growth in consumer borrowing. Low unemployment and rising confidence have increased consumers' appetite for big-ticket purchases and made lenders more willing to dole out larger loan balances while increasing credit-card limits. The boost to loan volume and balances has helped lift bank earnings and overall retail sales.

The shift to nonmortgage debt, including credit cards and personal loans, carries some drawbacks for consumers, including higher interest rates."This type of debt is problematic," said Cris deRitis, senior director and economist at Moody's ### invalid font number 2
Analytics### invalid font number 1
. "It should really be temporary," but for some consumers "it can be hard to get off that treadmill."

The boom in nonhousing consumer debt is in partthe result of lingering effects of the last recession that have kept many consumers from buying homes.

Some banks are tightening lending requirements in an effort to get in front of any problems. Discover's chief executive, David Nelms, said in October that the bank lowered spending limits on some of its credit cards and it is scaling back on some of the personal loans that it is originating.

The average charge-off rate for eight of the largest U.S. credit-card lenders by purchase volume was 3.25% in the fourth quarter, up 0.36 percentage point from a year earlier, marking the seventh straight quarter of year-over-year increases, according to Fitch Ratings.

Already, the average repayment period for loans issued to buy new cars hit a high of 69 months, according to third-quarter data from credit-reporting firm Experian PLC. Personal loans extended to consumers during the first half of last year averaged $6,285, according to the latest data from credit-reporting firm TransUnion, up 7.8% from a year earlier.

There cannot be many nonagenarians who spend their time worrying about cryptocurrencies.

But Charlie Munger, the 94-year-old billionaire investor, is convinced that bitcoin is oxious poison

 regard the bitcoin craze as totally asinine,e told a packed hotel ballroom in downtown Los Angeles, where hundreds of what he called roupiesathered for what some feared may be one of their final chances to question Warren Buffett long-trusted deputy.

Over the course of two hours at the annual meeting of his company the Daily Journal, Mr Munger sat back in his chair to field questions about oil and climate change, airlines and electric cars, how to make money and how to live a successful life.

It was a miniature version of the annual meeting of Berkshire Hathaway, where Mr Munger is vice-chairman. But whereas at Berkshire Mr Munger acts as the laconic sidekick to Mr Buffett, here he had the floor to himself.

And he was not short on criticism of aspects of the modern world.

Concerns over credit as Beijing's grip grows uncomfortably tight### invalid font number 1


Henny Sender

Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa

English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

INSIDE BUSINESS ASIA

V isitors to the Hangzhou head office of Ant Financial are usually invited to contemplate a huge digital display with flashing lights that conveys the latest information on the financial unit of ecommerce group Alibaba, and update the figures for loans across China to small and medium companies and to individuals.

Among the data not displayed, however, is the amount the group is borrowing on China's Rmb1.53tn asset-backed securities market, where it is the largest borrower, accounting for 13.7 per cent of the total, according to UBS data. But Ant is not immune from the regulators' scrutiny. China's mounting debt has long raised alarms and prompted predictions of the sort of financial collapse that seized the US and was quickly transmitted around the world a decade ago.

The more immediate concern is that the regulatory response to widespread acknowledgment of financial risk could lead to an over-tightening. That, in turn, could trigger exactly the sort of crisis the authorities are trying to forestall. It could also impact on the wider economy as smaller companies are denied the credit they need to grow.

Not long ago, the regulatory approach was to encourage innovation, enabling new fintech companies to lend to smaller businesses and households given the reluctance of conservative state-owned banks to do so.

Only 40 per cent of 1bn Chinese adults have a credit history with a traditional financial institution, says Jason Bedford, an analyst at UBS in The speed and intensity of the clampdown has surprised many platforms Hong Kong. He adds that at the end of October, peer-topeer lenders accounted for Rmb 1.2tn in outstanding loans, up from almost zero in 2014. But regulators are tightening their grip in the face of mounting evidence of fraud and the fear of a new kind of circular debt, freezing the activity of those lenders that have not already been closed.

As the year of the dog approaches, internet finance companies have been asked to engage in a seasonal campaign of "self inspection", another indication of China's changing regulatory landscape.

Executives at Shenzhen-based Ping An, an insurer that evolved first into a financial conglomerate and then a leader in ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 and ### invalid font number 2
big data### invalid font number 1
, fear that their borrowers are receiving money simply to pay back other lenders. To avoid that, they spend considerable time compiling information from the phone and WeChat habits of their customers. But few internet finance companies have risk management capabilities even remotely comparable to those of Ping An.

'Regulators used to be reactive.

Nowthey're The speed and intensity of the clampdown has taken many internet financial platforms by surprise. For example, Shanghai-based Dianrong, which has investors including one of Alibaba founder Jack Ma's family offices, the private equity completely proactive' arm of Standard Chartered and the Government of Singapore Investment Corp, has not been allowed to do much since last June, says one of its founders, Kevin Guo. That makes it unlikely the company will be able to go public any time soon, as its founders had hoped.

"The regulators used to be reactive," says Mr Guo. "Now they have become completely proactive."

Some worry that the potential damage stemming from the credit crunch for small businesses and the shuttering of numerous fintech institutions could lead to something far worse: a miscalculation that could trigger panic and contagion affecting high-quality financial companies as well.

For example, at one point last year regulators barred Anbang Insurance from selling wealth management products disguised as insurance policies. But they soon reversed course when they realised that if Anbang couldn't fund itself it would collapse, giving rise to even greater difficulties.

This was supposed to be a year in which a new generation of companies at the intersection of finance and technology would command ever higher valuations. So far, indications suggest that those hopes are likely to prove over-optimistic.

Mirae Asset Global to Acquire U.S. ETF Issuer Global X; U.S. funds will retain the Global X name### invalid font number 1


By Asjylyn Loder 
355 words

Corrections & Amplifications

With the acquisition of Global X, Mirae Asset Global Investment will have $30 billion in ETF assets under management. An earlier version of this story incorrectly said the assets of Mirae Asset Global Investments, the asset-management business of Mirae Asset Financial Group, had assets of $30 billion. (Feb. 14)

Global X, a U.S. ETF issuer with $10.2 billion in assets as of Jan. 31, is being acquired by Mirae Asset Global Investments, both companies announced Tuesday.

Terms of the deal weren't disclosed. Mirae will acquire complete control of Global X, including the minority stake purchased by JPMorgan Chase & Co. in 2016.

With the acquisition of Global X, Mirae, a financial firm based in Seoul, will have $30 billion in ETF assets under management.

Mirae will also gain a presence in the fast-growing $3 trillion U.S. ETF market, adding to its Horizons ETF lineup in Canada, BetaShares in Australia and Tiger ETFs in Asia. The U.S. funds will retain the Global X name.

Global X's ETF assets have more than doubled in the past year, according to FactSet. Its largest fund is the $2.1 billion Global X Robotics & ### invalid font number 2
Artificial Intelligence### invalid font number 1
 ETF, which trades under the ticker BOTZ.

"There are not a lot of independent ETF providers out there, and certainly not ones growing at this fast a pace," said Todd Rosenbluth, head of ETF and mutual fund research for CFRA, a New York research firm. "Global X has positioned themselves well to benefit from thematic investing, and they've had a couple of superstar products."

At current asset levels and prices, Global X's annual revenue is about $57.6 million, according to data compiled by FactSet.

ETF firms generally sell for eight to 12 times annual revenue, said Dave Nadig, chief executive officer of ETF.com. That would put Global X's sale price at about $460 million to $690 million.

"I could not be more delighted to find a partner such as Mirae Asset to bring this business to its next stage of its growth," said Bruno del Ama, co-founder of Global X, in a press release announcing the deal.

Hayashi's Take: Mulvaney Under Spotlight on Equifax Probe

Trump Administration Proposes Fees for Futures Industry

CFPB's Mission Overhauled; White House Proposes Budget Cuts

Equifax Hires Jamil Farshchi as Chief Information Security Officer

Market Volatility Strikes Exchange-Traded Products, Alarming Investors and Regulators

Mulvaney Under Spotlight on Equifax Probe

Mick Mulvaney, acting director of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, has been under fire from Democrats and consumer advocates since a news report this month suggested that the agency had pulled back from its investigation into last year's Equifax Inc. data breach.

CFPB officials have declined to clarify the bureau's position, citing the need to stay quiet about investigative matters. Expect to get some clarity from the agency chief this week.

Mr. Mulvaney, who is also the White House budget director, is scheduled to testify before the Senate and House budget committees Tuesday and Wednesday, respectively. While his main topic is the Trump administration's 2019 budget plan, he will likely be grilled by Democrats over changes to the CFPB, including the status of the Equifax probe.

Last week, over 30 Democratic senators signed a letter to Mr. Mulvaney, expressing concerns about the Reuters report that the CFPB may have halted its probe into Equifax.

On Sunday, Mr. Mulvaney gave some hints on CBS's "Face the Nation," suggesting the agency might be taking a different approach to the probe rather than ditching it.

Mr. Mulvaney urged people to watch for Equifax's coming 10-Q quarterly disclosure form to the Securities and Exchange Commission to check for changes in the investigations it faces. In the previous form released in November, the credit-rating firm cited the CFPB as among a long list of federal and state agencies looking into the data breach.

He then emphasized the bureau's priorities in consumer protection hadn't changed since he took over in November. "We want to run the place with a good deal of humility and prudence," he said. "We are not being aggressive. We are not pushing the envelope. We are taking a different attitude toward the job but our priorities have not changed."

"Not pushing the envelope," an expression Mr. Mulvaney has used repeatedly in recent weeks to describe the CFPB's new enforcement stance, may hold the key to understanding the Equifax probe.

Experts familiar with the investigation say that while the CFPB certainly has jurisdiction over the credit-reporting industry, there is a question about whether it has the authority to investigate data-breach cases specifically. The Federal Trade Commission is the main cop on that beat.

In Sunday's interview, Mr. Mulvaney pledged the CFPB would protect consumers from "fraud, unfair and deceptive trade practices, things that are illegal." What that entails will become clearer when the agency gives more guidance on its Equifax probe.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Trump Administration Proposes Fees for Futures Industry

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTONhe White House on Monday proposed levying fees on derivatives users to bolster the Commodity Futures Trading Commission's 2019 budget, an idea that may be dead on arrival because of industry opposition.

Moreover, leaders at the CFTC also are opposed to the funding idea.

The Trump administration's fiscal year 2019 budget calls for keeping the CFTC's budget appropriations flat at $250 million, but would add an additional $31.5 million in funding from a fee on some firms that participate in derivatives markets. The CFTC currently lacks the authority to collect such feesnlike its larger market regulator counterpart, the Securities and Exchange Commissionnd would require Congress to act to change its funding rules.

The budget proposal is a compromise with CFTC Chairman J. Christopher Giancarlo, a Trump-appointed Republican, who has been lobbying for additional funding but has publicly opposed fees on derivatives transactions. At a House Appropriations Committee hearing last year, he argued that market participants must already pay transaction fees to self-regulatory organizations and that additional costs could have a negative impact on liquidity.

"As the chairman has long maintained, a budget of $281.5 million will allow the Commission to effectively accomplish its mission, by providing increased resources for economic analysis, examinations of clearinghouses, cybersecurity, fintech, and enforcement," said CFTC spokeswoman Erica Richardson.

The fees would be assessed on derivatives users for activities such as product registration and reimbursing regulators for examinations and supervision.

CFTC discussions with the White House kept new fees out of the administration's 2018 budget request, according to people familiar with the matter. But disagreements over the proper level of funding for the agency led the CFTC to submit its own budget request to Congress, alongside the White House's request to keep the agency stuck at $250 million for the fourth straight year. Congressional Republicans largely vowed to ignore the CFTC's request, which Mr. Giancarlo said was necessary to fund a new technology initiative and hire more economists, among other priorities.

Industry giants such as CME Group Inc. have long opposed CFTC user fees. As the dominant futures exchange, CME has raised its own internal user fees several times in recent years, but has pushed back on proposals to fund the CFTC with similar levies.

Relying on user fees to fund the CFTC is "not a smart thing to do," said Terry Duffy, CME's chairman and CEO, at a 2014 Senate hearing. At the time, Congress was considering a proposal to fund the CFTC in the same fashion as the SEC, where congressional appropriations are fully offset by fees.

CME declined to comment Monday.

The 700-employee CFTC has repeatedly pressed for more funding, citing its expanded responsibilities from the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act.

CFPB's Mission Overhauled; White House Proposes Budget Cuts

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTONhe Trump administration on Monday further detailed its vision to change the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, proposing that the regulator get less funding and fewer enforcement powers along with an overhauled mission that is friendlier to companies.

In the CFPB's new five-year strategic plan, the administration articulated the thinking behind the changes in its enforcement and rulemaking policies being pursued under Mick Mulvaney, a longtime CFPB critic now serving as its interim director.

Separately, the White House budget plan unveiled Monday proposed sharply reducing the CFPB's budget for the next two years and restricting the agency's enforcement authority to "prevent actions that unduly burden the financial industry and consumer choice."

The budget plan calls for capping the CFPB's budget at $485 million for fiscal year 2019, which starts this Octobern amount equivalent to the 2015 levelnd $610 million for fiscal 2020. The CFPB's final budget requested under its Obama-appointed leadership was $630.4 million for fiscal 2018.

The White House said the two-year "restructuring period" would help impose "financial discipline" and "reduce wasteful spending."

In the latest strategic plan's introduction, Mr. Mulvaney wrote that the bureau is committed to fulfilling its responsibilities set under the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law that established the CFPB in 2011, but it would "go no further."

He said "pushing the envelope" would no longer happen, voicing implicit criticism of the agency's Obama-era leadership that used novel legal interpretations and tactics to oversee the financial industry. The CFPB's aggressive approach under former Director Richard Cordray was praised by Democrats and consumer advocates but often created friction with financial companies that saw the agency as overreaching.

Mr. Mulvaney has implemented various changes at the agency, including reconsidering an Obama-era regulation affecting payday lenders and shelving some enforcement cases. He also recently requested a $0 budget for the January-March quarter, saying the bureau would draw down reserves instead.

The administration's budget plan also aims to subject the agency to a legislative overhaul starting in fiscal 2020, and bring its budget under congressional oversight. The CFPB is an independent agency that is currently funded by the Federal Reserve.

"Trump's budget proposal would harm American consumers, hurt our nation's communities and wipe out financial reforms that are in place to prevent another financial crisis," Rep. Maxine Waters, the top Democrat on the House Financial Services Committee, said in a statement.

The shift in the agency's stance was spelled out throughout the 16-page strategic document.

Under the new plan, the No.1 goal for the bureau is to ensure that "all consumers have access to markets for consumer financial products and services." In the previous plan published in 2013, the priority was to "prevent financial harm to consumers while promoting good practices that benefit them."

The new vision seeks "Free, innovative, competitive, and transparent consumer finance markets where the rights of all parties are protected by the rule of law." That contrasts with the previous vision for an agency "that helps consumers finance markets work by making rules more effective, by consistently and fairly enforcing those rules, and by empowering consumers to take more control over their economic lives."

Equifax Hires Jamil Farshchi as Chief Information Security Officer

By AnnaMaria Andriotis

Equifax Inc. said it has hired a new chief information security officer to help with the company's overhaul of its security in the wake of its massive breach last year.

Equifax said Monday that it hired Jamil Farshchi, as its new CISO. In this role, Mr. Farshchi will be responsible for transforming the company's information security. He was previously CISO at Home Depot Inc. and held similar roles at Time Warner Inc. and Visa Inc.

He will report to the company's chief executive Paulino do Rego Barros Jr. who currently holds that position on an interim basis.

Mr. Barros said in a company statement that Equifax is confident Mr. Farshchi "possesses the talent and skill set needed to continue our journey toward developing industry-leading security practices and, ultimately, to help us regain trust with consumers and customers."

The company in September disclosed that it had been breached and that personal information for 145.5 million Americans had been compromised. A few weeks later, several executives, including the then chief security officer and chief information officer, retired. The company's then chief Richard Smith retired soon after, too.

Mr. Farshchi became CISO at Home Depot in 2015, several months after the company confirmed a massive breach to its payment systems. That breach resulted in stolen email and credit-card information that impacted millions of customers.

Before that, Mr. Farshchi was the global chief information security officer at Time Warner. He was also vice president of global information security at card network Visa where he led efforts on mobile security and ### invalid font number 2
big data### invalid font number 1
, according to the Equifax announcement.

"Equifax is a company with tremendous potential, and I am confident that we will transform our security program into one of the most advanced and recognized globally," said Mr. Farshchi in the statement announcing his appointment.

Market Volatility Strikes Exchange-Traded Products, Alarming Investors and Regulators

By Asjylyn Loder and Dave Michaels

The recent implosion of two exchange-traded products is renewing questions about the impact of fast-growing passive funds on the markets they are meant to track.

While exchange-traded funds have lowered the cost of investing and given individuals access to strategies once reserved for hedge funds and multibillion-dollar pensions, the $5 trillion global industry has ventured into complex strategies, sometimes with disastrous results.

The latest example came on the evening of Feb. 5, as ETPs that bet against Wall Street's fear gauge lost more than 80% of their value. The strategy has been a popular moneymaker in recent years as stocks marched steadily higher, keeping the Cboe Volatility Index, known as the VIX, at near-record lows.

That calm was shattered when the S&P 500 plummeted 4.1%, erasing nearly $1 trillion in market capitalization. The VIX surged, triggering steep losses in ETPs that had bet that the VIX would fall.

The next morning, Japanese securities firm Nomura Holdings Inc. and Swiss bank Credit Suisse Group AG said they would close two ETPs, the Nomura S&P 500 VIX Inverse ETN and the VelocityShares Daily Inverse VIX Short Term ETN, respectively. U.S. exchanges temporarily halted trading in similar ETPs.

The Securities and Exchange Commission has started examining the market mayhem, calling Credit Suisse on Tuesday and questioning the bank about its ETP, according to two people familiar with the matter.

Regulatory officials asked about how the investment's performance is calculated and whether retail investors were shareholders, one of the people said. The bank, which said it would shut down the ETP on Feb. 21, declined to comment.

Early indications are that the short-volatility products performed exactly as they were designed to amid Monday's market swings, but that is little comfort to investors who watched their money vanish.

Nick Ravo, a 62-year-old investor in Edmonds, Wash., said he had been betting against volatility for nearly two years and it has been largely profitable. He owned the Credit Suisse product in his retirement account on Feb. 5 and watched as the product lost nearly all of its value overnight.

Regulators have raised questions before about whether VIX exchange-traded products are suitable for retail investors. Wells Fargo & Co. agreed in October to pay more than $3.4 million to settle claims that it sold volatility-linked ETPs to customers who didn't understand them. The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority said Wells Fargo made the products available to retail customers without restriction from 2009 to 2012, and some clients "incurred significant losses."

"You will see more pressure on clearer and cleaner disclosure of complex products, and more scrutiny on firms about how they train their representatives to responsibly sell them," said Michael Osnato, a former senior enforcement official at the SEC now in private practice at Simpson Thacher & Bartlett LLP.

The big ETF firms moved quickly to distance themselves from the products that foundered last week. BlackRock Inc., the largest U.S. ETF firm by assets, published a blog post Wednesday from Martin Small, head of the firm's U.S. iShares ETF business, warning investors not to confuse its funds with the complicated products that were nearly wiped out Monday night.

"As the recent market volatility made clear, there's a big difference between plain vanilla ETFs and leveraged products making big bets with big risks," Mr. Small wrote.

In recent years, investors and analysts have blamed ETFs for roiling markets for oil, gold-mining companies and high-yield debt. Last year, a flood of cash into a triple-leveraged ETF triggered price gyrations in gold stocks from Toronto to Sydney. In September 2016, the Bank of Japan curbed its purchases of ETFs pegged to the Nikkei 225 index after its buying distorted prices. Some investors say it is a matter of time before that happens again.

"When ETFs become so large relative to the size of the market, they can have a big impact on the way up and the way down," said Peter Tchir, macro-investing strategist at Academy Securities LLC in New York.

Deutsche Bank to Pay $4.4 Million Over Traders Who Misled on Bonds

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTONDeutsche Bank AG agreed to pay more than $4.4 million to settle regulatory claims that it failed to supervise traders who misled customers about the price of commercial mortgage bonds.

The Securities and Exchange Commission said Monday that the payment includes $3.7 million to reimburse clients that traded with the German bank, which also will pay a $750,000 civil penalty. Deutsche Bank's traders generated illicit profits by misleading customers from 2011 to 2015 about the prices they paid to acquire the securities, which allowed them to sell the bonds at higher prices, the SEC said.

The bank's former global head of securitized products trading, Ben Solomon, agreed to a one-year ban from the securities industry and will pay a $165,000 penalty, the SEC said. Mr. Solomon, 42 years old, was fired over the alleged misconduct in August 2015.

'"Deutsche Bank and Solomon failed to keep watch as traders generated profits for the firm at the expense of CMBS customers by misrepresenting purchase prices and other important details," said Daniel Michael, the head of the SEC's complex financial instruments unit.

A spokesman for Deutsche Bank said the bank "cooperated extensively with the SEC's investigation and took appropriate disciplinary action, including termination in some instances."

Barclays Hit by New Charge Over 2008 Emergency Fundraising

By Max Colchester

LONDONarclays BCS 0.47% PLC was charged for a second time by U.K. authorities over a criminal case relating to an emergency fundraising in 2008 from Middle Eastern investors.

The Serious Fraud Office levied charges of unlawful financial assistance against Barclays' operating company Monday, a move that ramps up pressure on the British lender ahead of a landmark trial.

Last summer U.K. authorities charged Barclays over a $3 billion loan it made to Middle Eastern investors as the bank struggled amid the financial crisis in 2008. The British prosecutors had said the funds were then plowed back into Barclays to recapitalize it. Barclays has previously denied the allegations.

Those initial charges were made against the bank's holding company. The new charge is significant as the operating company, Barclays Bank PLC, holds the lender's banking licenses. Regulators must deem the subsidiary to be "fit and proper" to keep those licenses. If found guilty, Barclays could, in theory, be at risk of losing its banking licenses.

In a statement Barclays said it would defend itself against the charges. The bank added that it didn't expect there to be any impact on its "ability to serve" customers. The bank hasn't said whether it would plead guilty or not against the charges.

Other European banks including France's BNP Paribas SA and Credit Suisse Group AG have in the past pleaded guilty to criminal charges without any lasting impact. Credit Suisse's settlement with U.S. authorities for aiding tax evasion didn't result in it losing any licenses. BNP Paribas was banned for a year from dollar clearing after pleading guilty to dealing with sanctioned countries. However, in the U.K. no major bank has recently been found guilty of a criminal charge, so the outcome of such a decision is unclear.

The SFO case has sucked in a cadre of former Barclays' executives who helped structure the deal at the height of the crisis which allowed the British bank to avoid being bailed out by taxpayers. The SFO has spent years probing how Barclays managed to woo Qatari investors to pump funds into the struggling bank. In particular what the purpose was of £22 million in "advisory services" that bank paid to Qatari investors.

British prosecutors last summer charged the former chief executive of Barclays with fraud and illegal payments along with three other former executives. It is the first time a chief executive of a major British bank faces criminal charges for actions taken during the financial crisis.

A date for the trial is expected to be set soon.

New Loans by Chinese Banks Hit Record High in January

By Lingling Wei

BEIJINGhinese banks logged a record amount of new loans in January, as the government's effort to contain financial risks caused a shift toward traditional lending from murkier forms of financing.

While credit growth slowed overall, new bank loans hit 2.9 trillion yuan ($461 billion) last month, up 43% from a year earlier, according to data released Monday by the central bank. Total social financing, a catchall term for various forms of credit that, in addition to bank loans includes bonds and other types of nonbank lending, increased 3.06 trillion yuan, a drop of 17% from the growth in the year-earlier period. Bank loans accounted for 88% of all new credit.

Rather than reflecting a new round of government stimulus, the unexpected increase in bank credit was primarily driven by banks bringing loans back onto their books to heed regulators' demands, economists and analysts say. At the same time, businesses also increased their appetite for bank loans as other forms of financing became harder to come by. In addition, Chinese banks tend to dole out more credit at the beginning of the year.

"This is an absolute monstrous number no matter how you look at it," said Christopher Balding, an economics professor at Peking University. The 2.9 trillion yuan in January lending represented almost 60% of all credit extended in 2008, when China was launching a large stimulus program to shield the economy from the global financial crisis.

The jump in bank loans was well above forecasts by economists polled by The Wall Street Journal for 2.15 trillion yuan and underscores policy makers' tough balancing act. Over the past two years, President Xi Jinping and his economic officials have made reducing ballooning debt levels a centerpiece of economic policy. But with the leadership pursuing a growth target many have viewed as too high, state companies and local governments have kept borrowing to expand.

Now, there is a realization within the government that it is in fact impossible for China to cut debt levels or cease relying on bank financing without hurting growth. As a result, the goal to deleverage, as written in the nation's economic plans for the past two years, has been softened into a mission to control the growth of debt this year.

In recent months, the central government has been calling a halt to new projects in the construction of subway systems all over the country, including two separate, $4 billion subway projects in Inner Mongolia.

Those and other measures aimed at controlling borrowing by localities could cause debt growth to slow this year, said Larry Hu, China economist at Macquarie Group. That, in turn, could lead to weaker economic expansion, he added.

"If policy makers still want to maintain growth, they have to boost government investment, which would then run counter to their deleveraging efforts," Mr. Hu said.

So far, authorities have focused efforts on clamping down on risks in the financial sector. In particular, they are targeting a rampant practice under which banks team up with outside financial firms to move loans off their balance sheets and then peddle them as high-yielding investments to individual savers. Such a strategyspecially popular at small and midsize banksas helped free up bank capital and made it easier to extend loan deadlines or new credit.

But that has created a parallel buildup of debt and made the financial system more vulnerable to shocks such as a liquidity crunch. To stem the risks, regulators since last year have been forcing banks to come clean on off-book lending, cut back on short-term borrowing and adopt more rigorous risk controls.

As these measures have caused banks to bring loans back onto their balance sheet, many lenders are starting to worry about a potential shortage of capital to cushion against potential loan losses. That is a big reason many Chinese banks are pushing back against a new proposal intended to control off-book lending.

"It's really tough for banks these days," said an executive at a national lender based in northern China.

hao Deng contributed to this article.

European Countries Woo London Bankers With Lower Taxes

By Max Colchester

Are you a London banker looking to move to a tax-friendly locale after Brexit?

How about&aris!

Some of Europe's most legendary tax-heavy countriesmong them France and Italyre cutting special deals to woo rich expats with the promise of smaller personal tax bills. (Read more about the tax breaks, here).

KPMG LLP calculated the personal tax bill of a hypothetical expatriate earning ¬50,000 ($306,000) a year in five European countriesrance, Spain, Ireland, Germany and the Netherlandsor The Wall Street Journal. The results show that in all of them the expat would pay less in income tax and social-security charges than he or she would in the U. K.

The savings aren't trivial. An expat living in France earning ¬50,000 could take home an extra ¬7,588 after tax and charges compared with one living in the U.K., according to the calculations. That extra pay climbs to ¬2,545 in Spain if all the tax breaks are applied. Even Germany, which doesn't have an expat regime, leaves the worker some ¬,500 better off than in Britain.

The findings counter a widely held view that the U.K. is an attractively taxed island in Europe's sea of suffocating taxes. "What is less well understood is how significant the incentives and other special treatments that some locations offer can be," said Christopher Cowell, a partner at KPMG.

London's legion of foreign financiers is taking note. International banks in the U.K. are forming plans to bulk up in cities across the EU so that they can continue selling products to clients based there after Brexit. Around 10,000 finance jobs in Britain may disappear when the U.K. quits the EU, according to an estimate by the Bank of England.

Across Europe, fiscal red carpets are being rolled out in preparation.

Take France. In 2012, the French president at the time, Françis Hollande, called finance the "enemy" and imposed a wealth tax. But soon after the U.K. voted for Brexit, Mr. Hollande's government extended rules that allow employees recruited abroad to claim 30% of their salary as an "impatriate premium," which is income-tax free for up to eight years. There are further tax rebates including those for days worked outside France. In total, expats based in France can get up to half of their salary exempted from income tax.

The French aren't alone. In Spain, expats pay a special 24% tax rate on earnings up to ¬00,000. The Dutch allow 30% of salary to be paid as an income-tax-free expatriate allowance. In Ireland, the tax-exemption deal extends to children's school fees. The Belgians allow expats to deduct the cost of decorating their new house from their tax bill. Last year, Italy unveiled a deal that doles out a 50% tax break to skilled workers coming from abroad. The U.K. by comparison doesn't offer many such generous income-tax breaks to foreigners domiciled there, advisers say.

Conditions and time limits apply to the European tax sweeteners. In France, for instance, to qualify you need to have lived abroad for the last five years. In Spain, you have to be paid from an entity outside the country to get a lower tax rate, which can prove tricky to structure. The Irish need expats to earn at least ¬5,000 a year to get the tax breaks. In the Netherlands, you can't have been living within 150 kilometers of the Dutch border within the last 16 months (which bizarrely means that some people residing on the U.K.'s south coast aren't eligible). Another issue to consider in the Netherlands is a cap on bonuses for bankers.

And to be sure the sweet deals are for personal tax, not corporate tax. The U.K.'s corporate tax rate is lower than, say, France's. Labor laws also are far more stringent in countries such as Germany than the U.K. That has made some companies think twice about relocating big chunks of business into the continent.

Finally, money isn't everything. Factors such as language and the availability of private-school seats also play an important role in deciding whether to move abroad. But the lower tax rate is making it easier to convince staffers to give up London for another European city, bank executives say.

"When I say o and live in Paris for a few years and pay less tax,' people suddenly aren't so skeptical," says one senior U.S. banker in London.

Nevada Regulator Starts Website to Field Tips About Steve Wynn

By Chris Kirkham and Kate O'Keeffe

Nevada gambling regulators introduced a new online system for the public to send in confidential complaints and tips after receiving a number of reports about Steve Wynn in the days after The Wall Street Journal published an article detailing sexual-misconduct allegations against the casino mogul.

Becky Harris, chairwoman of the Nevada Gaming Control Board, said the goal of the system is to have "a more efficient, more effective way for the public to communicate with the board." Ms. Harris, who became chairwoman on Jan. 22, four days before the Journal article on Mr. Wynn, said it quickly became apparent that an upgrade was needed, based on the deluge of phone calls, emails and traditional mail.

"It was disruptive," she said. "It was a challenge to conduct the necessary business of the board."

Michael Weaver, a spokesman for Wynn Resorts Ltd. and Mr. Wynn, said the company had no comment.

The new online system, which went live Monday afternoon on the gaming control board's website, is expected to allow anyone who wants to send information to leave comments that will be directed to the appropriate investigators. Ms. Harris said the site would be checked regularly and if there are additional questions, "people can expect to receive a returned phone call."

All communications will remain confidential in the system.

While the idea resulted from the volume of calls related to Mr. Wynn, Ms. Harris said, the online system is meant to be a channel for any case the board is investigating. "This is a process that will remain in place from this point forward," she said.

The Las Vegas Metropolitan Police Department, which has also received multiple complaints about Mr. Wynn in the wake of the Journal story, according to a person familiar with the matter, is coordinating with the gambling regulator and running point on any reports that could potentially involve criminal allegations.

A spokesman for the department said in a Monday statement that it has received two sexual-assault complaints against Mr. Wynn since the Journal investigation was published. One woman made a report from St. Louis, alleging that Mr. Wynn sexually assaulted her in Las Vegas in the 1970s, according to the police department spokesman, Larry Hadfield. He declined to elaborate on the woman's allegations and added that the police couldn't move forward with the investigation since it wasn't reported within the 20-year statute of limitations for sexual assault.

A second woman filed a report a week later in person at a police substation in Las Vegas, alleging that Mr. Wynn had assaulted her in Chicago, also in the 1970s, the spokesman said. He declined to elaborate on that complaint, either, and added that Las Vegas police took a courtesy report and will forward the information to Chicago authorities.

Mr. Wynn stepped down last week as chairman and chief executive of Wynn Resorts, citing "an avalanche of negative publicity" in the wake of the Journal investigation.

Dozens of people the Journal interviewed who have worked at Mr. Wynn's casinos told of behavior that cumulatively would amount to a decadeslong pattern of sexual misconduct by Mr. Wynn, including pressuring employees to perform sex acts. Mr. Wynn has blamed his ex-wife, Elaine Wynn, for instigating the allegations and said, "The idea that I ever assaulted any woman is preposterous."

Ms. Wynn has consistently declined to speak to the Journal. An attorney for Ms. Wynn has said the notion she instigated the Journal's article "is just not true."

The Wynns remain embroiled in long-running litigation over restrictions on her right to sell her 9% stake in Wynn Resorts.

Gambling regulators in Nevada and Massachusetts, where Wynn Resorts is building a $2.4 billion casino, have opened investigations since the Journal first reported the allegations in late January, and both have said they would continue those probes despite Mr. Wynn's resignation last week.

Mr. Wynn still has a 12% stake in Wynn Resorts and as a result is subject to Nevada casino licensing regulations.

At a public meeting last week, Massachusetts gambling officials raised questions with implications for Wynn Resorts board members and other executives who could have been in a position to know about Mr. Wynn's alleged misdeeds and didn't report them.

The Wynn Resorts board pledged to investigate the allegations against Mr. Wynn, but last week it cut ties with an outside law firm, O'Melveny & Myers LLP, that had been hired just days earlier to assist. In a statement, the board's "special committee" to investigate the allegations said it "no longer required the services" of the law firm after Mr. Wynn resigned. On Monday the special committee announced it was giving the assignment to a new law firm, Gibson, Dunn & Crutcher LLP, which has longstanding connections to the casino company.

Mr. Weaver, the Wynn Resorts corporate spokesman who is also representing the board and the special committee, didn't answer questions about why Mr. Wynn's departure led to the board's decision to terminate the outside law firm's investigation. He also didn't say what the board committee would be investigating, or how they would be conducting the investigation, beyond a statement that the investigation "will be detailed and thorough."

Company officials have been reaching out to some former female employees as part of an investigation the company itself is conducting, separate from the board's, according to people with knowledge of the matter. One former Wynn executive, Kevin Tourek, was brought on to work with the company in December, after the Journal started reaching out to former employees about Mr. Wynn's behavior, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Mr. Tourek has been making calls to former employees as part of the company's investigation, according to people briefed on the matter.

Mr. Tourek didn't return calls requesting comment.

Mr. Weaver said Mr. Tourek is working with the company but declined to answer additional questions about Mr. Tourek's role.

Bitcoin's Energy Drain

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/rural-washington-is-a-hot-spot-for-bitcoin-miners-1518354001" to generate new units of cryptocurrencies process that requires vast amounts of electricity to run and cool thousands of machines. That has become a dilemma for utilities that are figuring out how to deal with the deluge of requests without bearing the brunt of wild swings in cryptocurrency prices.

Mining operations can squeeze into small spaces, and they run servers constantly, which can strain electrical systems that aren't built for such heavy use. If all the cryptocurrency mining operations in the works go forward, power demand could double in some areas and require expensive new infrastructure.

There are already at least 30 known cryptocurrency-mining operations in Chelan, Grant and Douglas counties in central Washington, which has some of the cheapest power in the U.S. Four of the mining inquiries that Chelan County's utility has received since October are for 100 megawatts each. That is enough power for more than 50 hospitals, and building the infrastructure for each mine could cost more than $40 million.

Stricter Bank Supervision Brings More Lending

Joã Granja and Christian Leuz analyze in "http://papers.nber.org/tmp/69625-w24168.pdf" how strict supervision of banks affects lending and local business activity. They study the impact of the elimination of the Office of Thrift Supervision in 2011 after the financial crisis and "find stricter supervision operates not only through capital but can also overcome frictions in bank management, leading to more lending and a reallocation of loans. Consistent with the latter, we find increases in business entry and exit in counties with greater expose to OTS banks."

Leave Bank Leverage Ratios Alone

Lawmakers who want to weaken banks' capital standards have forgotten the lessons of the 2008 financial crisis, Sheila Bair writes in a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/congress-flirts-with-disaster-on-bank-leverage-ratios-1518479068". Ms. Bair, who was head of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. from 2006 to 2011, argues against relaxing the "modest constraint on leverage" called the supplemental leverage ratio. "It would be the height of irresponsibility for Congress to loosen capital requirements now, when big banks need to prepare for rough times that may lie ahead," she writes.

Fannie-Freddie Overhaul Plan Puts Shareholders at Risk

A Senate plan to dismantle government-sponsored mortgage-finance giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac and shift its business to the private sector could take 10 years or more, during which time stockholders "would remain in financial limbo," Tim Pagliara of CapWealth Advisors LLC writes in a "https://morningconsult.com/opinions/corker-warner-bill-would-perpetuate-fannie-and-freddies-limbo/". A "more prudent course" would continue efforts to reduce Fannie and Freddie's investment portfolios and let them restore capital reserves, he says. "While this course of action alone would not forever shield taxpayers from having to cover losses during severe downturns, it would be far less risk than making the taxpayer responsible for all the mortgage-backed securities the still-unknown successors to [Fannie and Freddie] would issue," he writes.

Deutsche Bank AG's U.S. head of global transaction banking, Paul Saltzman, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/deutsche-banks-u-s-head-of-global-transaction-banking-is-leaving-1518464122", according to an internal memo from the head of the unit, which provides trade financing and manages cash for companies and governments.

All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial ServicesAuthority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

Single price: Based onamid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge fromfund managers/operators.

Our staff are shocked and upset by the scandal### invalid font number 1


CHRIS FLOOD

INTERVIEW

Client trust badly hit by the Wells Fargo bogus accounts affair, admits Nico Marais bogus accounts affair, admits

Nicolaas Marais is under no illusions about the scale of the task confronting Wells Fargo.

He joined the US bank's $504bn asset management arm in February 2017. This was after the hugely damaging admission by Wells, the third-largest US lender, that its staff had created up to 3.5m bogus customer accounts to meet sales targets.

That scandal led to the departure of the chief executive John Stumpf, a boardroom shake-up and the imposition this month of unprecedented sanctions by the Federal Reserve, which barred the bank from increasing its asset pool until governance improvements are made.

Mr Marais, known as Nico, describes colleagues as "shocked and upset " by the scandal.

The asset management unit was not involved in the fraud and is not restricted from growing its assets but Mr Marais readily acknowledges the daunting challenge of repairing trust among clients.

"We will have to work very hard, client by client, mandate by mandate. But talking about this is not enough," says the 54-year old.

"We know that we have to prove our worth to clients."

Mr Marais adds that Wells Fargo's $1.9tn broader wealth and investment management division, of which WFAM is a part, has continued to grow in spite of the problems at the parent bank.

A veteran who has worked for almost three decades in finance, Mr Marais started his career as a gold trader for South Africa's central bank before moving on to a series of investment roles for the World Bank. He joined Barclays Global Investors in 2000, the business that was sold by the UK bank in late 2009 to Black-Rock, the world's largest asset manager.

It was while working at BGI that Mr Marais first met Kristi Mitchem, who was appointed chief executive of WFAM in April 2016.

Ms Mitchem later played an important role in persuading Mr Marais to move from Schroders, where he oversaw $118bn as head of multi-asset investments and portfolio solutions.

Speaking during a recent visit to London, he is generous in his praise of Ms Mitchem, describing her as "one of the most dynamic leaders I have ever worked with", and insisting that he has never before enjoyed such a "positive work culture".

"The lack of politics has been refreshing," he says. "Decision-making can happen at lightning speed at the management team level because we trust each other."

WFAM employs more than 500 investment professionals, spread across 29 autonomous teams. But Mr Marais boils asset management down to three essential functions that respond to the needs of his own family.

"We protect wealth, that's my 87-year-old dad, or we grow wealth, that's my 21-year-old son, or we pay income. That's it," he says, with a mild Johannesburg accent.

Wells Fargo moved its asset management activities into a separate unit within its broader wealth and investment management division as part of a re-organisation in 2015.

But in spite of strongly favourable market movements over the past two years, assets run by WFAM have increased by just $24bn since the 2015 re-organisation, as some clients have chosen to switch into low-cost passive funds.

Along with every US money manager, WFAM is facing intense competition from the leading passive fund providers BlackRock and Vanguard, which has led to downward pressure on fees across the entire investment industry.

"Asset managers historically have been quite insulated from some of the price competition that we see across other industries. But there is nothing wrong with fee pressure. It is just a question of how to structure a business to deal with that challenge," he says.

Along with his role as head of multiasset class solutions, Mr Marais is also responsible for overseeing new product research and development, an area where he believes WFAM should focus on "thought leadership" rather than following rivals that have rushed into launching "me too" low-cost index-tracking funds.

"I'm in the jewellery business, not the mining industry," he says, adding that a hard division between active management strategies and passive index trackers is "not really the point" for most investors.

"I'm agnostic about the choice between active versus passive as long as the strategy serves what the client wants," he says.

WFAM has been expanding its quantitative investment capabilities following the acquisition of the Analytic Investors business in 2016. More data scientists have been hired to work alongside investment staff. This month WFAM hired Wai Lee, previously head of quantitative investments at Neuberger Berman, as global head of research in the multi-asset solutions team led by Mr Marais.

"We want to take the fusion between quantitative techniques and fundamental analysis to the next level, to explore how ### invalid font number 2
big data### invalid font number 1
 and ### invalid font number 2
machine learning### invalid font number 1
 can translate into real value for a stockpicker," he says.

As president, Mr Marais oversees WFAM's international operations so he is watching the progress of the Brexit negotiations between the UK and EU with some care.

"I hope that sanity will prevail but we have to plan in the face of uncertainty. The UK will always remain a very important market to Wells Fargo Asset Management but if we have to build more capacity in Europe after Brexit, that is what we will do," Mr Marais says.

Hannon's Take: Carney Sees Rise In Natural Rate
