Carney Sees Rise In Natural Rate

One of the features of a surprisingly strong 2017 for the global economy is that investment spending appears to have picked up, having long been the element of demand that had recovered most slowly from the financial crisis.

That in turn may feed in to one of the questions that investors are pondering as 2018 gets under way: are central bank policy rates set to rise more rapidly than they, and indeed policy makers, had expected?

Bank of England Gov. Mark Carney is one who thinks they might.

Backing up a bit, as my colleague Greg Ip explains, one of Janet Yellen's legacies as chairwoman of the Federal Reserve is a lower assessment of the equilibrium rate of interest, also known to central bankers as the natural or neutral rate. That is the rate which is low enough to keep the economy growing and unemployment low but high enough to maintain stable inflation.

This is the rate that balances investment and savings, and part of the reason it may have fallen is that the former appears to have been weakened by the crisis, while the latter has been boosted by an aging population.

But if investment spending is now picking up, so might the equilibrium rate.

"One of the things that is possible over the course of the next few years is that the underlying global equilibrium interest rate could be increasing," Mr. Carney said in a news conference, having warned that the BOE will likely raise its key rate sooner and further than it had previously expected to.

A higher equilibrium rate would mean that central banks would have to raise their own policy rates further if they intend to remove stimulus.

"If you stay still in that environment, then all things being equal, you're providing more stimulus," Mr. Carney said.

Key Developments Around the World

Rand Paul Says He'll Oppose Goodfriend's Fed Nomination

By David Harrison

Sen. Rand Paul (R., Ky.) said he would vote against the nomination of Marvin Goodfriend for a spot on the Federal Reserve's board of governors, raising the prospect the Carnegie Mellon University economist might not have enough support to win confirmation.

"I'll be a no," Mr. Paul said Thursday.

Earlier Thursday, the Senate Banking Committee voted along party lines to advance Mr. Goodfriend's nomination.

Mr. Goodfriend hasn't attracted any Democratic support. If no Democrat or independent votes to confirm him, the GOP can lose no more than one of its votes in the Senate. Republicans have a 51-49 majority in the chamber, with 47 Democrats and two independents. The GOP's thin majority has been even more delicate due to the medical absence of Sen. John McCain (R., Ariz.), who is being treated for brain cancer.

Last year, Vice President Mike Pence broke a 50-50 tie to secure confirmation for a handful of nominations where Republicans joined Democrats in opposition.

It is unusual for the Senate to reject a president's Fed nominees, especially when the president's party controls the Senate as it does now. In 2011, Peter Diamond, a Nobel Prize-winning economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, withdrew his name from consideration to become a Fed governor after some Republicans on the Senate Banking Committee opposed his nomination.

President Donald Trump nominated Mr. Goodfriend in November. The White House's other Fed nominees, Chairman Jerome Powell and Randal Quarles, vice chairman for supervision, won Senate confirmation with bipartisan support.

Mr. Trump stands behind his nomination of Mr. Goodfriend, "who is incredibly qualified," said White House spokeswoman Lindsay Walters in a statement Thursday.

Mr. Paul said he was troubled by Mr. Goodfriend's past support for tracking cash as it moves in and out of banks.

In academic papers, Mr. Goodfriend has supported pushing interest rates below zero during recessions and advocated charging a fee to take cash out of banks to make negative rates more effective. He has suggested inserting a magnetic strip on bank notes to track them as they enter circulation.

Mr. Paul's father, former Rep. Ron Paul, was a vocal opponent of the idea. On Thursday, the younger Mr. Paul indicated he shared his father's views on placing magnetic strips on bank notes.

"That doesn't sound very exciting to me," he said.

Before Thursday's 13-12 committee vote to advance Mr. Goodfriend's nomination, Sen. Sherrod Brown (D., Ohio) said he was troubled by the economist's concern about rising inflation in the aftermath of the financial crisis. He also said he was concerned that Mr. Goodfriend had paid "lip service" to the Fed's independence.

"It ultimately rang hollow," Mr. Brown said.

Democrats pressed Mr. Goodfriend during his confirmation hearing last month about the accuracy of his economic predictions. In particular, they criticized him for calling on the Fed to raise rates after the financial crisis, warning that inflation could rise dangerously high if it didn't. Inflation has undershot the Fed's 2% target for much of the past five years.

After the hearing, analysts said Mr. Goodfriend may have hurt his chances at confirmation not because of a poor forecasting recordany economists, including at the Fed, have made such errors in recent yearsut because he didn't provide an accounting for why his projections had proved off the mark.

Mr. Goodfriend has also urged more congressional oversight on the Fed and said the Fed should adopt a mathematical target to set monetary policy.

At his confirmation hearing in January, Mr. Goodfriend said a policy rule would help enhance Fed transparency, but insisted he is committed to the central bank's independence. When asked whether he thought the Fed should have followed his advice to begin raising interest rates in 2012, he said, "No, I don't," but added that his remarks were being taken out of context.

Fed's Kashkari Says January Jobs Report t Best Mixed' on Wages

By Kate Davidson

Minneapolis Federal Reserve President Neel Kashkari said Thursday that last month's employment report was "at best mixed" on the outlook for wage gains and said he wants to see more data that rising wages are translating to inflation before the Fed raises short-term interest rates again.

"If wages start increasing on a consistent basis around the country and that fact translates into higher prices, in inflation, I would expect to see that will affect how the Federal Reserve approaches interest rates and that could affect the trajectory for interest rates, but we are a long way away from that," he said at a town hall forum in Pierre, S.D.

The Labor Department said last week that average hourly wages grew 2.9% from a year ago in January, the biggest gain since the recession.

Mr. Kashkari said the report provided early evidence that wages may be starting to go up nationally, but said policy makers still need to see more data showing those gains will lead to higher prices.

"I don't want to rush to judgment," he said. "I want to look at the data and actually make decisions based on the data."

Mr. Kashkari was one of two Fed officials who voted against the Fed's decision to raise its benchmark federal-funds rate in December to a range between 1.25% and 1.5%. Officials have penciled in around three rate increases in 2018 and will meet next March 20-21, when markets expect they will raise rates again.

Mr. Kashkari isn't a voting member of the Fed's policy-setting Federal Open Market Committee this year.

Fed's Kaplan Reinforces His Case for Three Rate Rises This Year

By Todd Buell

FRANKFURT-nemployment in the U.S. will drop below 4% and 2018 will be a "strong" year for America, the president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas said Thursday, reinforcing his base case for three interest-rate rises this year.

"We think we're already at or near full employment, we would expect headline unemployment to dip below 4% during this year," Robert Kaplan said.

Speaking on U.S. monetary policy, he said, "I still believe that it is the right plan for us to remove accommodation in a gradual, patient, but deliberate manner."

"My own base case is three [rate rises]," Mr. Kaplan said, but added this wasn't a "static estimate."

"I'm watching financial conditions, economic conditions, the labor market, and progress on inflation," he told reporters on the margins of an event at Germany's Bundesbank in Frankfurt.

"While I say I have a base case, I'm always learning and trying to understand what's going on and be vigilant," he added. Mr. Kaplan doesn't have a vote on monetary policy this year.

The Fed has been gradually nudging up interest rates in recent years, removing monetary stimulus as the unemployment rate has fallen to 4.1%. A statement released after the Fed's latest meeting on Jan. 31 offered nothing to dispel market expectations that the central bank would deliver its next rate increase in March.

Fed officials are hoping to keep rates low enough to encourage inflation to firm up a bit without surging out of control, amid a tight labor market and solid economic growth. Inflation measured by Fed's preferred measure rose 1.7% in December.

He reiterated comments made Wednesday suggesting that he isn't terribly concerned about recent market volatility.

"At this point, I don't see this market adjustment spilling over into other financial conditions, but I'll be watching, carefully," he told reporters. He said he was more accustomed in his career in finance to higher volatility. "The period that's been more abnormal in my career has been the last 15 months."

om Fairless contributed to this article.

Fed's Harker Says If Data Supports It, March Rate Rise Is Possible

By Michael S. Derby

Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia President Patrick Harker said Thursday that while he foresees slightly fewer rate rises this year than many of his colleagues, a case may exist to raise rates at the next central bank policy meeting.

"I would be open to a March increase" in the Fed's overnight short-term target rate range, Mr. Harker told reporters after a speech in New York, referencing the upcoming Federal Open Market Committee policy meeting scheduled for March 20-21.

Mr. Harker said the case for a potential move higher is a balancing act. He said the Fed shouldn't move so fast it dampens growth and complicates its effort to get inflation back to the 2% target. But it also needs to move interest rates back toward more neutral levels and gain some capacity to cut rates again should the economy run into trouble again.

Mr. Harker, who is not a voting member of the FOMC this year, said his outlook for monetary policy is a hair different than the current consensus. "I still have penciled in two [2018 rate rises], because I would like to see us slightly overshoot our 2% inflation target. But I think there are some risks to the upside where I would be open to three going forward." He added what happens with rates depends on the economic data, especially in regards to price inflation.

Based on the Fed's last forecasts from its December policy meeting, most officials are eyeing around three rate rises this year, in what's now a target set between 1.25% and 1.50%. Mr. Harker and other Fed officials don't believe a recent passed tax overhaul bill will add much to growth, at least in a way that would change the rate outlook.

Mr. Harker also said the heavy-duty volatility in financial markets over recent days hasn't altered his view on the outlook for monetary policy and the economy.

"I'm not going to tell the market if it's healthy or unhealthy" to have seen asset prices fall, Mr. Harker said. But he's not entirely surprised.

He said long-term bond yields are rising because market participants are taking on board that Washington's current policy regime will mean big deficits and an increase in government borrowing, boosting supply of debt securities. He added, "If you start to believe the long end of the curve is going to go up, it makes sense equities would have an adjustment."

Other Fed officials have been sanguine about the market's woes as well. New York Fed President William Dudley said on Wednesday "my outlook hasn't changed because the stock market is a little bit lower than it was a few days ago. It's still up sharply from where it was a year ago." He added, "having a bump like this has virtually no consequence in my view to the economic outlook."

In Mr. Harker's speech before a gathering of college educators in New York, he said economic growth is under pressure from a long-running trend of declining labor force participation.

The drop in the labor force "is largely due to demographic factors," Mr. Harker said. But even so, growth "is likely to suffer" without something that reverses the trend. "Simply put, we need more people," Mr. Harker said.

He also worried about borrowing to attend college. "The overall issue of student debt is important because it doesn't just affect individuals, it resonates across generational lines in families and spreads into the wider economy," Mr. Harker said. "On the national level, research by the Philadelphia Fed's Consumer Finance Institute, along with others, shows that student debt is affecting both small business formation and homeownership rates," he said.

Fed's Dudley Says Chance of a March Rate Increase Looks Good--Bloomberg TV

By Michael S. Derby

Federal Reserve Bank of New York leader William Dudley said in a television interview Thursday that prospects for a March rate rise are looking good, as he was upbeat about the economy and not concerned about the heavy volatility on Wall Street.

When it comes to boosting short-term borrowing costs at the March 20-21 Federal Open Market Committee meeting, "as long as I'm comfortable the economy is going to continue to grow at an above trend pace at a time when I think monetary policy is accommodative and financial conditions are easy, I'm probably going to be supportive of removing monetary policy accommodation," Mr. Dudley told Bloomberg's television channel.

"I think there many things on the side of the economy being stronger than expected" rather than weaker, so that makes the chance of boosting the current overnight target rate range of between 1.25% and 1.50% quite possible, he said.

"But again, let's see where we are at the time of the meeting" before actually making the call on rates, Mr. Dudley said. The official also said the central bank's current outlook, which has penciled in three rate rises for 2018, is "reasonable," though he added that how the economy performs will be the key to what happens.

Mr. Dudley, who is set to retire this summer, is a highly influential Fed official who also serves as vice chairman of the FOMC. The interview with Mr. Dudley occurred in the midst of highly volatile trading in stock markets, as investors have moved to reprice securities to reflect the possibility of more inflation and what some see as increasing odds the Fed may have to raise rates more than expected.

So far, Fed officials have shrugged off the market's woes and done nothing thus far to justify fears the central bank may raise rates more than the three increases officials projected back in December. Indeed, some in the Fed have appeared to welcome of the stock market's declines in a climate where central bankers were already viewing asset prices as elevated.

In an appearance Wednesday, Mr. Dudley said "my outlook hasn't changed because the stock market is a little bit lower than it was a few days ago. It's still up sharply from where it was a year ago." He added, "having a bump like this has virtually no consequence in my view to the economic outlook."

In a separate appearance on Wednesday, Dallas Fed President Robert Kaplan said, "I think it's healthy that there is some correction, a little more volatility in markets." He added that he still favors raising rates in a "patient and gradual manner."

That same theme was echoed earlier Thursday when Philadelphia Fed chief Patrick Harker spoke with reporters in New York. He too was leaving unrevised his economic outlook, saying, "I still have penciled in two [2018 rate rises], because I would like to see us slightly overshoot our 2% inflation target. But I think there are some risks to the upside where I would be open to three going forward." He added what happens with rates depends on the economic data, especially in regards to price inflation.

In his comments to Bloomberg, Mr. Dudley said the market selloff is in large part driven by market participants accepting the reality of a strong global economy and what that means for central banks. He said long-term bond yields are on the rise as market participants embrace an outlook that's likely to bring a series of rate rises.

When it comes to the stock selloff, "if it stops here the implications for the economic outlook are very marginal," Mr. Dudley said. He described the degree of market selloff as "small potatoes."

WSJ Survey: Economists Expect Three Fed Rate Increases in 2018

By David Harrison and Ben Leubsdorf

Forecasters see the U.S. economy gathering steam this year and the Federal Reserve raising short-term interest rates three or perhaps four times by the end of 2018.

Economists surveyed in recent days by The Wall Street Journal on average predicted U.S. gross domestic product would rise 2.8% in 2018, accelerating from 2.5% growth in the fourth quarter of 2017 versus a year earlier, supported by the recent package of tax-code changes.

They also projected the unemployment rate would fall below 4% by midyear, from 4.1% in January.

The economists predicted the Fed would raise rates at its next meeting, March 20-21, followed by another move at its June 12-13 meeting.

The central bank in December raised its benchmark federal-funds rate to a range between 1.25% and 1.5%. The economists' average expectation was for the rate to end the year at 2.21%. That's consistent with Fed officials' anticipation of three quarter-percentage-point increases this year, which would lift it to a range between 2% and 2.25%.

The survey respondents' projections have been inching up as their economic forecasts have grown stronger in recent months. In November, they saw the fed-funds rate ending 2018 at 2.07%.

David Berson, chief economist at Nationwide Insurance, said the Fed was "on the borderline between three-four moves this year."

The latest survey of 63 business, financial and academic economists was conducted Feb. 2-6, after the release of the January jobs report and during a period of stock-market volatility.

Despite the Wall Street turbulence, the economists remained optimistic about the economy and anticipated the Fed would stick with its plan of gradually raising interest rates under new Chairman Jerome Powell. Some said the chances that officials would move more aggressively than anticipated outweighed the chances they would move less aggressively.

Gus Faucher, chief economist at PNC Financial Services Group, said the Fed under Mr. Powell would maintain "continued gradual tightening."

The average probability of a recession in the next year was 14%, ticking up from 13% in January's survey but remaining low. Nearly two-thirds of forecasters said they saw more risk that the economy would grow faster than it would grow more slowly, another sign of optimism about the outlook.

"The tax-cut stimulus and faster wage and salary growth are the garlic to the vampire of recession," said Sean Snaith, director of the University of Central Florida's Institute for Economic Competitiveness.

One reason several days of swinging stock prices didn't rattle forecasters: 59% said they thought U.S. stocks were overvalued, and several others said stocks had been overpriced before their recent decline.

"February's drop has pushed prices more in line with earnings," said Lynn Reaser of Point Loma Nazarene University.

The economy has shown signs of strength over the past few months. On Friday, the Labor Department reported average hourly earnings for private-sector workers were up 2.9% in January over the previous year, the largest such gain since 2009.

In theory, continued growth and low unemployment should put upward pressure on inflation, which has undershot the Fed's 2% target for much of the past five years. Stronger inflation could lead the Fed to pencil in a faster pace of rate increases.

The central bank's preferred inflation index was 1.7% higher in December than a year earlier. Investors, anticipating stronger inflation, have pushed the yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note above 2.8%; it was close to 2% in September.

Several Fed officials have said they could support four rate increases this year if the economy picks up steam.

"The issues of whether we should have three or four rate hikes this year is really going to be driven by the data and changes in the outlook," San Francisco Fed President John Williams said Friday. "Both of those kind of possibilities are reasonable to think about as options."

On Wednesday, New York Fed President William Dudley said the past few days of market volatility haven't affected his view of the economy.

"Having a bump like this has virtually no consequence, in my view, to the economic outlook," he said.

ichael S. Derby contributed to this article.

WSJ Survey: Economists Support Fed's 2% Inflation Target

By David Harrison

Federal Reserve officials in recent months have floated ways they might alter the central bank's 2% inflation goal. But economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal have a message for the Fed: Don't touch that target.

About 84% of economists said the Fed should stick with its current target, in large part to avoid damaging the central bank's credibility.

"Changing the rules could trigger skepticism and uncertainty," said Lynn Reaser, an economist at Point Loma Nazarene University in San Diego.

The Fed adopted the 2% target in 2012. Now, some officials, including former Chairman Ben Bernanke, say the central bank should examine alternatives that would better help the economy recover from recessions in an era of persistently lower inflation and interest rates.

Some have suggested a so-called price level target in which policy makers would try to let inflation rise faster in good times to compensate for weaker price increases during downturns. Another option would be to target nominal growth in gross domestic product instead of inflation. There has also been talk of raising the inflation target above 2%.

"The costs of hitting the effective lower bound for a prolonged period should cause a reassessment of the 2% inflation target," said Boston Fed President Eric Rosengren in January.

Many other developed country central banks have adopted similar 2% inflation targets. Economists in the survey said it made sense for the U.S. to match the goals of its peers. Allen Sinai, chief economist at Decision Economics Inc., said the 2% goal is the "accepted standard world-wide and an expected inflation anchor if central banks stick to it."

Inflation has undershot the target for all but two months in the past five years.

That suggests officials would have an even harder time hitting their mark if they raised the target or if they sought to let inflation run high for a time to make up for weak inflation periods.

"It's like moving the goal post when you can't put the ball in the net," said Rajeev Dhawan, director of the Economic Forecasting Center at Georgia State University.

Just 11% of economists surveyed supported moving to a price-level target and 5% were in favor of raising the inflation target.

Some economists said that while they support sticking with the 2% goal, they would be open to including a range between readings slightly below and above the target. David Shulman, senior economist at UCLA Anderson Forecast, suggested a range of between 1.5% and 2.5%.

Others said the Fed should try instead to hold prices steady or limit inflation to 1%.

Officials have said their target is "symmetrical," meaning they would tolerate inflation running slightly above or slightly below it.

Mexico's Central Bank Lifts Rates to Nine-Year High

By Juan Montes

MEXICO CITYhe Bank of Mexico lifted interest rates by a quarter percentage point Thursday to a nine-year high, amid stubbornly high inflation and investor expectations that the U.S. Federal Reserve may raise rates faster than previously thought.

The overnight interest rate target now stands at 7.5%, the highest since February 2009. The move was expected by most economists and follows a similar move in December.

In its policy statement, the central bank left the door open to additional rate increases, saying it "will act in a timely, firm way to keep inflation expectations anchored."

The decision was unanimous. It was the first policy meeting attended by Irene Espinosa, who was appointed last month as the first woman at the bank's five-member board. It was the second meeting for the Bank of Mexico chief Alejandro Díz de Leó, who took the governor's post in December.

Some analysts said the newcomers are probably seeking to assure investors of their commitment to fighting inflation.

"We suspect that today's hike was the final move in the tightening cycle, but given recent swings in currency markets it clearly won't take much to persuade policy makers to move again," said Neil Shearing, chief emerging-markets economist at Capital Economics.

Normally, the bank probably would have stayed on hold, "but a combination of Nafta concerns, a new governor trying to burnish his inflation-fighting credentials with markets, and recent swings in the global financial markets all point towards playing it safe," he said, referring to the North American Free Trade Agreement that the U.S., Mexico and Canada are renegotiating.

The peso, which is he second-most-traded emerging-market currency after the Chinese yuan, weakened slightly after the announcement ashe quarter-percentage-point rate increase had been widely discounted. The peso traded in Mexico City at 18.9450 to the U.S. dollar Thursday afternoon, already weakened by the decline in global stock markets.

Mexico's annual inflation eased in January to 5.55%, from 6.77% in December, but it is still well above the central bank's 3% target. The central bank thinks inflation will return to the target in the first quarter of 2019, taking longer than previously expected.

The Bank of Mexico has raised interest rates by 4.5 percentage points since December 2015. Higher interest rates, which lead to more expensive credit and less consumption, are one factor behind the economic slowdown that Mexico experienced last year. Economic output expanded 2.1% in 2017, according to preliminary data, the lowest growth in four years.

The big beneficiaries have been banks and savers. Private banks, among them the Mexican subsidiaries of U.S. Citigroup and Spain's BBVA, had profits of $7.3 billion last year, a 28% increase from 2016, Mexico's banking regulator recently said.

In the last two years, consumer prices have been hit by several shocks. The peso has depreciated 22% since early 2015 due to falling oil pricesome 17% of Mexico's government revenue comes from oilnd uncertainty related to the renegotiation of Nafta. A weak peso makes imports more expensive, affecting end prices.

In early 2017, the government ordered an unprecedented 20% increase in gasoline prices, which in turn pushed up transportation costs.

The Mexican peso has recovered some ground in recent weeks as fears about a possible collapse of Nafta talks dissipated. But recent positive economic data in the U.S. have prompted investors to flee from riskier assets such as the peso, betting the Fed will raise interest rates faster than initially expected.

The Bank of Mexico said Thursday that the peso could face new episodes of volatility in the coming months as uncertainty remains high. It cited Mexico's presidential elections in July; the Fed's policy stance; and the risks that Mexico, the U.S. and Canada ultimately fail to reach a deal on Nafta.

Russia Cuts Key Interest Rate as Inflation Falls Below Target

By James Marson

MOSCOWhe Bank of Russia cut its key lending rate to 7.5% from 7.75% Friday, citing annual inflation that has fallen below the bank's target level of 4% and decreasing expectations of price rises.

The bank said in a statement that it would likely continue cutting the key rate as "the possibility of annual inflation rising above 4% this year has significantly decreased."

Inflation stood at 2.2% in January, a record low for the post-Soviet period.

The bank said economic growth slowed in the final quarter of 2017, although it was unclear why. Russia's economy returned to growth last year after a two-year recession caused by weak prices for oil and Western sanctions.

Bank of Canada's Wilkins Flags Concern Over Technology Firms' Market Concentration

By Kim Mackrael

OTTAWAhe market dominance of the world's largest technology firms could hurt competition and lead to higher prices if policy makers don't intervene, Bank of Canada Senior Deputy Governor Carolyn Wilkins said Thursday.

In a speech delivered to the G-7 Symposium on Innovation and Inclusive Growth, Ms. Wilkins said the five biggest technology companies have a market capitalization that, at $3.5 trillion, is about one-fifth the size of the U.S. economy. While the industry makes an important contribution to economic growth, she said in prepared remarks, the size and dominance of some firms raises concerns about a possible monopoly on prices and competition.

Ms. Wilkins said user data has become an additional source of power that can make some large companies "virtually unassailable." New technologies such as algorithmic pricing could also lead to tacit price collusion, she said.

The central bank official said governments should prioritize the modernization of antitrust and competition policy to ensure new companies can compete. "We are not going to get the full benefits of innovation if we leave market power unchecked," she said.

Ms. Wilkins also called on policy makers to work on improving workers' skills so they can benefit from innovation and to manage the risks that third-party digital service providers may pose to the financial system.

Ms. Wilkins didn't comment on Canada's economic outlook in her prepared remarks.

Last month, the Bank of Canada raised its benchmark interest rate to 1.25%, the third increase since mid-2017. The central bank said at the time that the economy was running roughly at capacity, but uncertainty over the North American Free Trade Agreement was clouding the economic outlook.

Stock Slides Likely Making Bank of Japan More Cautious About Rates

By Megumi Fujikawa

TOKYOhe Bank of Japan is likely more cautious about changing monetary policy after stocks plunged this week, an adviser to Prime Minister Shinzo Abe said, as he called on the bank to keep supporting the economy aggressively.

"If Japan rushes to exit from monetary easing, after this big turmoil, it could be swallowed in a wave of global stock falls," Koichi Hamada, one of the architects of Abenomics, said in an interview with The Wall Street Journal.

The Yale University economics professor emeritus said many central banks around the world may now be thinking twice about the appropriate speed for tightening policy.

"The Federal Reserve may not want to raise interest rates immediately after such a huge move in the stock market. If so, it would delay monetary tightening in Europe and push back the possibility of rate increases in Japan even further," he said.

The comments come in week that has seen the Dow Jones Industrial Average plunge more than 1,000 points on two separate days and entering correction territory, with falls partly stoked by fears over rising Treasury yields and higher interest rates. The Nikkei Stock Average marked its biggest point drop since 1990 on Tuesday. The sharp falls served as an indication of investors' heightened sensitivity to rising bond yields and policy moves.

Speculation that Japan's central bank might raise interest rates later this year has grown since Mr. Kuroda in November highlighted the potential drawbacks of superlow interest rates. Japan is in one of its best growth spurts in decades, prices are rising again and central banks around the world, led by the Fed, are tightening policy as the global economy gains speed.

Mr. Hamada lauded the success of the Abenomics project so far, but said it was important to keep plugging away at it and not change course even with a different BOJ governor.

Mr. Kuroda's aggressive quantitative easing program, now tweaked to focus on interest rates, has been one of the key pillars of Abenomics. Policy makers say it has helped spur the longest economic expansion in 16 years and the tightest job market in more than four decades.

The prime minister said earlier this week that he trusted Mr. Kuroda's abilities and praised him for changing market sentiment for the better, but he still hasn't given a clear indication of whether he intends to reappoint Mr. Kuroda. Market participants expect he will remain at the post.

Mr. Hamada said it wasn't necessary that Mr. Kuroda continued as governor provided his successor continued the same aggressive quantitative easing and yield curve control that has helped improve job conditions and corporate profits.

Still, the fact that inflation hasn't reached 2% means that the labor markethich he said was ultimately more important than the 2% figuresn't stretched enough to justify any rate increases.

"The key for increases in wages and prices is whether average payments to full-time workershich have been struggling to riseill start turning upward. If they do and cause cost-push inflation, that's when the Bank of Japan may start thinking of looking for the exit to QE," he said.

BOJ's Suzuki Says Cumulative Impact of Low Rates Needs to be Watched

By Megumi Fujikawa

TOKYOBank of Japan policy board member Hitoshi Suzuki said Thursday the bank must pay attention to the impact of low interest rates on the country's financial system, and should continue the current ultra-easy monetary policy to support further price increases.

"It is necessary to carefully watch cumulative impact of prolonged monetary easing on financial system and banks' intermediation functions," Mr. Suzuki said in a speech.

The former executive at the Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi UFJ said Japan's financial system has been stable thanks to banks' strong capital, although low lending rates have squeezed their earnings.

The comments are in line with a view previously expressed by Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda in a speech at the University of Zurich last year, that the negative effects of low rates can increase over time.

In the speech, Mr. Kuroda referred to the reversal ratehe point at which low rates set by a central bank starts to hurt the banking industry and undermine the benefits of loose monetary policy. The remark sparked speculation in markets about rate increases later this year.

While Mr. Suzuki also agreed with the governor's view that inflation is still only halfway to the bank's 2% target and the BOJ should continue its easing program, he sounded more upbeat on the price outlook.

"Conditions are becoming ready for the pace of price increases to speed up further" thanks to the tight labor market, as well as the government's push for higher wages and improving productivity, he said.

The central bank expects inflation to reach 2% by March 2020. In December, core inflation rose 0.9% on year, matching the pace marked in the previous month.

RBA Warns Full Employment Still Some Way Off

By James Glynn

SYDNEYustralia will lag well behind the global shift toward higher interest rates, with the central bank Friday forecasting tepid inflation for some years yet, while warning full employment will be illusive.

The Reserve Bank of Australia forecast core inflation will drift up only slowly, reaching the lower end of its 2-3% target band by mid-2019, and just 2.25% by mid-2019. Core inflation is critical to policy-making at the RBA.

Economic growth will be stronger, running at a healthy 3.5% by mid-2019, but unemployment will fall only slowly.

The RBA lowered its forecast for unemployment to 5.25% by the end of 2018, from a forecast of 5.5% in November. The jobless rate is currently 5.5%.

But the outlook sees unemployment remaining above 5.0%, a level the RBA has associated with full-employment.

"The unemployment rate is set to remain above estimates of full employment for some time yet," the RBA said in a quarterly report.

The outlook is consistent with comments made Thursday by RBA Gov. Philip Lowe that there is no case for interest rates to be changed in the near-term.

"The Reserve Bank Board doesn't see a strong case for a near-term adjustment in monetary policy," Mr. Lowe told a business forum overnight.

The RBA has held its cash rate target at a record low 1.5% since mid-2016, and some economists expect the pause could extend well into 2019 or beyond.

Mr. Lowe also made it clear this week the RBA feels no pressure to follow its global counterparts like the U.S. Federal Reserve in raising interest rates, saying Australia's floating exchange rate gives it flexibility to make policy based on domestic conditions.

Despite the general caution, the RBA said 2018 sees the economy on a more solid footing than a year earlier, with the job market adding more than 400,000 new positions in the last 12 months.

The biggest uncertainty before the RBA is weak wages growth. Languishing at recession-style 20-year lows, policy makers are patiently waiting for further tightening of the job market to convert into higher wages.

So far there has been wage pressures reported in only small pockets of the labor market.

A recovery in wages growth will be needed if the RBA is to achieve its goal of 2-3% inflation.

The same stamina that has pushed Larry Contrella to complete marathons also has brought him success in landing deals at software-focused JMI Equity.

The 31-year old principal stood out early on when he was interviewing back in 2009 to become part of a three-person associate class at the Baltimore-based midmarket firm, said General Partner David Greenberg. At the time, Mr. Contrella was training for the New York City Marathon while maintaining the grueling hours required of an investment banking analyst. He joined JMI's Baltimore office in 2010.

"We were excited about his potential," Mr. Greenberg said. "The thing in the interview process we learned that proved to be part of his work personality is he's a very committed and fast runner, and that he is very disciplined."

Larry Contrella

Background Before joining JMI Equity, Mr. Contrella worked as an investment banking analyst at Merrill Lynch & Co. He earned his bachelor's degree in economics at the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School.

Select Investments Benevity Inc., CoreHR Ltd., Higher Logic LLC, Level Accessormerly SSB BART Groupnd Schoology Inc.

Relationships Mr. Contrella and Marco Schnabl, co-chief executive officer of automotiveMastermind Inc., keep in touch following the sale of JMI's stake in the company last year. They have seen a business partnership turn into a friendship. Mr. Schnabl said they would take a skiing trip to Jackson Hole this monthust as they did last yearo mark the exit but not the end of their relationship.

That drive has helped Mr. Contrella become one of the most productive members of JMI's investment team, completing six acquisitions in the last three years, Mr. Greenberg said. He also worked on exits of the firm's stakes in automotiveMastermind Inc. and DoubleVerify Inc. during the same period. IHS Markit Ltd. and Providence Equity Partners, respectively, acquired majority stakes in the companies last year.

"The last three years, Larry has done more deals here than anybody," Mr. Greenberg said. "He has no trouble telling you what the challenges are. That's definitely a big area of growth for him."

That fast and furious merger-and-acquisition pace was borne partly out of the lessons Mr. Contrella learned from his early days at the firm. When he first started, Mr. Contrella worked on JMI's internet team at a time of increasing risk to companies' business models from technology giants, Mr. Greenberg said.

"He saw a lot of risks come to fruition," said Mr. Greenberg, adding that since that time, Mr. Contrella has taken feedback about risk into account when investing in companies and has had no problem putting money to work.

Among Mr. Contrella's noteworthy deals was JMI's 2016 investment in automotiveMastermind, a provider of predictive ### invalid font number 2
analytics### invalid font number 1
 and marketing-automation software for the auto industry.

Marco Schnabl, co-founder and co-chief executive officer of automotiveMastermind, said Mr. Contrella's intelligence, humility and ability to put others at ease helped win the team over when the company was seeking its first round of institutional capital. AutomotiveMastermind's meeting with Mr. Contrella was the first of 20 the management team had with prospective investors. However, throughout the interviewing process, JMI and Mr. Contrella stood out.

"The first meeting was an intro meeting with Larry," Mr. Schnabl said. "It felt very natural. He's a humble, smart, high-energy person, and professional. We knew from the very first moment we were extremely comfortable."

The early relationship with Mr. Contrella blossomed during the company's short tenure within JMI's portfolio. Mr. Contrella said that during JMI's holding period, the business increased its head count to 220 employees from 70 members. Mr. Schnabl added that Mr. Contrella also was key in helping the company field an offer from IHS Markit, while explaining to them that they didn't need to feel pressured to take any deal. IHS Markit ultimately bought a 78% stake in the company for about $392 million. At the time of the deal, IHS Markit said it would buy the remaining 22% stake if the company hit certain financial milestones. Mr. Schnabl said Mr. Contrella helped negotiate a deal that preserved automotiveMastermind's culture, which was important to the CEO.

He added that Mr. Contrella also helped put management at ease during a time when automotiveMastermind was below its growth target, right after JMI invested in the company.

"I turned to Larry and Dave and said, his will be an embarrassing month for us, I feel terrible,'" Mr. Schnabl said. "Their feedback was basically, ey guys, we believe in you. Don't worryhis is the long run for us.' Everyone at the company knows that story."

European buyout groups struggle to attract investors### invalid font number 1


ALIYA RAM

Brexit talks uncertainty behind caution as foreign funds boost tech sector

European technology start-ups raised record levels of funding last year after a series of blockbuster deals led by foreign investors, but many of the continent's venture capital firms struggled to raise money, according to just released data.

Non-European investors were responsible for the biggest deals with SoftBank, the Japanese technology group, leading a ¬58m financing of UK virtual simulation technology start-up Improbable, and US funds T Rowe Price and Fidelity investing $385m into Deliveroo, the London-based takeaway food app. Chinese ecommerce group JD.com also boosted the figures with its $397m investment in Farfetch, the luxury goods website.

However, research from Pitchbook, a data provider, shows the total capital raised by Europe's venture groups fell by a quarter to ¬.4bn amid growing uncertainty about the outcome of Britain's negotiations to leave the EU. Despite large venture capital firms, such as Balderton Capital, closing big fundraising rounds, the total number of new funds dropped to a 10-year low of 54 in 2017, compared with 75 the previous year.

"If you're looking to invest into a UK or European fund, Brexit is a factor that you didn't have to consider before," says Doug Trafelet, managing director at Pitchbook. "You may say, 'Why not wait for a couple of months before investing in that fund?' ... or just invest 80 per cent of what you were going to invest."

The tougher fundraising environment for investors follows two years in which money poured in to Europe's venture capital groups.

Enthusiasm for the continent's technology sector specially in areas such as ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 and financial technology ovetailed with low interest rates, to attract pension funds, insurance companies and family offices on the hunt for returns.

The excitement was fuelled by an increase in the amount of money allocated to the European Investment Fund, a public-private partnership created by the EU to support venture capital in the bloc. The initiative has become a central plank for the bloc's venture capital groups and accounts for more than a third of investment in UK-based funds.

Between 2014 and 2015, after the European Investment Bank decided to increase the EIF's capacity, venture capital groups saw a near-doubling in fundraising to ¬.4bn.

However, following the UK's 2016 vote to quit the EU, the EIF paused its investments in venture capital funds based in the UK, casting a pall over smaller investors that depend on it for capital. "The new funds are having a harder time raising money, or the amount [they raise] will be smaller," says Omri Benayoun, general partner at Partech Ventures, which shelved plans to open an office in London until the outcome of the UK-EU negotiations.

Venture capital companies are considered a key part of the technology "ecosystem", that enables start-ups to raise money and advice from experienced investors. Foreign investors and big companies have instead driven growth.

As big automotive and retail companies attempt to modernise their businesses, data from Atomico, the Londonbased venture capital company, show 637 financing deals last year involved at least one corporate investor, a 19 per cent increase on the year before. Asian investors meanwhile put $1.8bn into start-ups in the continent, compared with just $876m in 2016.

Certain pockets of the industry have attracted outsized interest. The data from Pitchbook show ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 start-ups raised ¬.2bn last year, more than double the previous year, while fintech companies raised ¬.35bn, more than triple the amount in 2016.

"European entrepreneurs are becoming more ambitious and more competent at building bigger and bigger companies," says Neil Rimer, co-founder at Index Ventures. "It's an onward march for tech in Europe."

Countries such as the UK and France have benefited from their expertise in these specific sectors. "When we started out most people were sceptical at the idea of trying to build a hard technology company in London, let alone an AI research company," says Demis Hassabis, co-founder of DeepMind, the ### invalid font number 2
artificial intelligence### invalid font number 1
 start-up bought by Google in 2014. "So eight years on it's pretty amazing to see how much support is now flowing into the sector." Venture capital investors argue that moving away from a large number of small funds and a high volume of smaller deals will benefit start-ups that have struggled to achieve the scale of their counterparts from Silicon Valley or China.

"The ecosystem has evolved in terms of the geographic reach and the emergence of venture capital groups outside the UK, in Germany, France, the Nordics and beyond," says Tom Wehmeier, a partner at Atomico. "What you see now riven in part by the sustained improvement in European Venture s a really strong willingness from corporate and private venture funds from insurance and other investors abroad to engage with Europe's tech community."
